The Hanafi Dispute on Claiming Non-Delivery in Iqrar of Price
Ruling on whether a buyer can claim non-delivery of a purchased slave when acknowledging the price, and the disagreement among Hanafis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules of iqrar to determine when a claim of non-delivery is accepted and identify the precise point of disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
In iqrar, a claim of non-receipt is accepted if the cause is confirmed; if denied, it requires connection.

This passage presents an internal Hanafi dispute regarding a buyer who acknowledges a debt for a purchased slave but claims he never received the slave. The ruling turns on how the seller (the muqarr lahu) responds to the buyer's claim. Understanding this case reveals the subtle interplay between iqrar (acknowledgment), jiha (cause), and qabd (delivery) in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
A buyer acknowledges a debt of 1000 dirhams as the price of a slave he bought, but claims he did not receive the slave. The seller may respond in different ways.
2
Parse the seller's possible responses
The seller can either confirm the cause (the sale) or deny it. He may also confirm or deny the claim of non-delivery. The ruling depends on these combinations.
The key is whether the cause (jiha) is affirmed or denied.
3
Apply Abu Hanifa's ruling
Abu Hanifa does not believe the buyer under any circumstance if the seller denies non-receipt, whether the cause is confirmed or denied. He sees the claim of non-delivery as a retraction of the acknowledgment.
Abu Hanifa treats the acknowledgment as absolute; the claim of non-delivery is contradictory.
4
Apply the Sahibayn's ruling
If the seller confirms the cause (sale), the buyer is believed even if the claim is disconnected; because the sale is established, the buyer's word on delivery is taken. If the seller denies the cause, the buyer is believed only if the claim is connected (part of the same utterance), as it becomes a modifying explanation (bayan mughayyir).
When cause denied, connection is required because it changes the nature of the acknowledgment.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the buyer must pay the full amount regardless. Under the Sahibayn, if the sale is admitted, the buyer does not have to pay until delivery is proven; if the sale is denied and the claim is connected, the buyer is also not liable.
The practical outcome differs in whether the burden of proof shifts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قال: لفلان عليَّ ألف درهم من ثمن جارية باعنيها، لكني لم أقبضها : لم يصدق عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله إذا كذبه المقر له في قوله : لم أقبضها، وصدقه في الجهة، أو كذَّبه في الجهة، وادعى المال. وقالا : إن صدقه في الجهة : صُدَّق وإِن فَصَل؛ لأنه إذا صدقه فيها: ثبت البيع، فيُقبل قولُ المشتري : إنه لم يقبض، وعلى المدعي البينة. وإن كذَّبه فيها : صُدِّق إذا وصل ؛ لأن هذا بيان مغيّر، من قبل أن
And if he says: 'I owe so-and-so one thousand dirhams as the price of a slave girl he sold to me, but I did not receive her,' he is not believed according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) if the acknowledgee denies his statement 'I did not receive her,' and he confirms the cause (jiha), or he denies the cause and claims the money. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If he confirms the cause, he is believed even if disconnected; because if he confirms it, the sale is established, so the buyer's statement that he did not receive is accepted, and the burden of proof is on the claimant. And if he denies the cause, he is believed if connected, because this is a modifying explanation.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage discusses a case where a person acknowledges a debt of 1000 dirhams as the price of a slave girl (jariya) that he claims he bought but did not receive (qabd). Abu Hanifa does not believe him if the creditor (muqarr lahu) denies the claim of non-receipt, whether or not he confirms the sale. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad distinguish: if the creditor confirms that the sale took place (i.e., he sold the slave), then the buyer's claim of non-delivery is accepted even if not connected (i.e., even if he separates the phrases), because the sale is established, and the buyer's word is taken regarding delivery, with the burden of proof on the creditor. If the creditor denies the sale (the cause), then the claim of non-delivery is only accepted if connected, because it becomes a modifying explanation (bayan mughayyir) that changes the nature of the acknowledgment from a debt to a claim for delivery. The text cuts off but the ruling is clear: the modalities depend on the creditor's response.

Key Terms
جهة (jiha): The cause or reason for the debt, e.g., sale, loan, etc.
بيان مغيّر (bayan mughayyir): A modifying explanation that changes the legal nature of the acknowledgment, e.g., from absolute debt to conditional on delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The buyer is not believed even if connected.
B) The buyer is believed only if connected.
C) The buyer is believed even if disconnected.
D) The seller's confirmation is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi iqrar, a claim of non-delivery can be accepted if the underlying sale is admitted, but if denied, it requires connection as a modifying explanation (bayan mughayyir).

Metaphorical Interpretation of Delivery Terms in Acknowledgment
Intra-Hanafi dispute on linguistic interpretation of delivery terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi criteria for determining whether a word can be used metaphorically (majaz) in legal contexts, and apply them to the dispute between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Words can shift from literal to metaphorical; the Hanafi school debates whether delivery terms extend to contracts.

In this passage, the discussion moves from the general principle of acknowledgment with wasl to a specific dispute between two great Hanafi imams: Muhammad al-Shaybani and Abu Yusuf. The question is whether the words 'dafa'a' (you gave) and 'naqada' (you paid) can be interpreted metaphorically to refer to a contract rather than actual physical delivery. This linguistic analysis is essential for usul al-fiqh, as it determines the ruling (hukm) in acknowledgment cases.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the two positions
Read the Arabic text and identify the scenario: someone acknowledges receiving money using 'dafa'a' or 'naqada', but claims he meant a contract, not physical receipt. Then note the two positions: Imam Muhammad accepts this interpretation (with wasl), Imam Abu Yusuf rejects it.
2
Identify Imam Muhammad's linguistic claim
Muhammad argues that 'naqd' and 'daf'' linguistically mean giving ('i'ta'), and giving can be used metaphorically for contracts. Therefore, these terms can also be used metaphorically.
He equates the terms with 'i'ta' to justify metaphor.
3
Identify Imam Abu Yusuf's linguistic objection
Abu Yusuf counters that 'dafa'a' and 'naqada' are specific to physical delivery and action (taslim wa fi'l), not to giving in a broad sense. He distinguishes them from 'i'ta', which is a gift and can be metaphorical.
He insists on the literal, specific meaning of these terms.
4
Distinguish between 'dafa'a'/'naqada' and 'i'ta'
Analyze the lexical scope: 'i'ta' is more general and includes transferring ownership, while 'dafa'a' and 'naqada' imply hand-to-hand delivery. Understand why this distinction matters for usul.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: According to Muhammad, a person using these words can be believed if he explains with wasl that he meant a contract. According to Abu Yusuf, he cannot be believed, and the acknowledgment is taken as physical delivery.
Both positions are valid within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قال: دفعت إليَّ عشرة دراهم، أو نقدتني، لكني لم أقبض: فكذلك عند محمد رحمه الله ؛ لأن النقد والدفع بمعنى الإعطاء لغةً، فيجوز أن يُستعار للعقد أيضاً. وقال أبو يوسف رحمه الله : لا يُصدَّق ؛ لأنهما اسمان مختصان للتسليم والفعل، وأما الإعطاء فهبة، فيصلح أن يُستعار للعقد.
And if he says: 'You gave me ten dirhams' or 'you paid me', but I did not take possession: then similarly with Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), because 'naqd' and 'daf'' linguistically mean giving, so it is permissible to use them metaphorically for the contract as well. And Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) said: He is not believed, because these two terms are specific to delivery and action, while 'i'ta'' (giving) is a gift, so it can be used metaphorically for the contract.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific application of the previous ruling. If someone acknowledges receipt using the words 'dafa'a' (you gave) or 'naqada' (you paid), but then claims he did not actually take possession and meant the contract, Imam Muhammad holds that he is believed with wasl because these words can be used metaphorically for a contract. Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that these terms are exclusive to physical delivery and action, whereas the word 'i'ta'' (giving) has the capacity for metaphorical use in contracts.

Key Terms
الدفع (al-daf'): Literally 'pushing' or 'giving'; in this context, it refers to handing over money.
النقد (al-naqd): Literally 'payment' or 'cash'; in this context, it refers to giving money in hand.
الإعطاء (al-i'ta'): Giving; a broader term that can include transferring ownership, distinguished by Abu Yusuf from 'dafa'a' and 'naqada'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Yusuf, why is a person not believed if he says 'you gave me' but then claims he meant a contract?
A) Because the term 'dafa'a' is ambiguous.
B) Because 'dafa'a' and 'naqada' are specific to physical delivery.
C) Because the person is lying.
D) Because the contract was not written.

Key Takeaway
The dispute between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf illustrates how linguistic precision in usul al-fiqh can lead to differing rulings even within the same madhhab.

Parsing an Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Acknowledging Dirhams as Loan and Claiming They Are Counterfeit
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a qualifying statement is a valid explanation or a retraction, illustrating usul principles of amm and majaz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a Hanafi usul dispute by identifying the linguistic categories (amm, khass, majaz) and applying the principle of ghalaba to determine whether a qualifying statement is a valid explanation or a retraction.

Core Idea
A general term does not include incidental defects; only a predominant attribute can be specified metaphorically.

This passage presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi disagreement on the interpretation of the term 'dirhams' in an acknowledgment. The core question is whether a claim that the dirhams are counterfeit is a permissible modifying explanation (bayan mughayyar) or an invalid retraction. The dispute turns on whether counterfeitness is considered an inherent subtype or an incidental defect, and how the predominance (ghalaba) of good dirhams affects the use of the general term. Let's parse the text to uncover the usul principles at work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Read the acknowledgment scenario: someone acknowledges dirhams as a loan or price, then adds 'they are counterfeit.' Note that the dispute is about the second statement's legal effect.
The first statement creates an obligation; the second modifies it.
2
Locate the Linguistic Categories
Identify the term 'dirhams' as a general noun (amm). Determine whether 'counterfeit' is a type (naw') or a defect ('aib). This classification is central.
Think about how predominant usage affects meaning.
3
Trace Each Imam's Reasoning
For Abu Hanifa: counterfeitness is an incidental defect ('arid 'aib), so the absolute name does not include it; the statement is a retraction. For the two: because good dirhams predominate, counterfeit becomes like a metaphorical specification; the statement is valid when connected.
The two rely on the usul concept of ghalaba (predominance leading to majaz).
4
Extract the Usul Principle
General terms (amm) are understood according to their predominant usage. If an attribute is not a recognized subtype within that predominant category, it cannot be used to specify the general term without changing the meaning—thus constituting a retraction.
Compare with other cases like claiming a term or option.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أقرَّ بالدراهم قرضاً، أو ثمن بيع، وقال: هي زيوف: صح عندهما موصولاً ؛ لأن الدراهم نوعان: جياد، وزيوف، إلا أن الجياد غالبةٌ، فصار الآخرُ كالمجاز، فصح التغيير إليه موصولاً. وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : لا يُقبل وإن وصل؛ لأن الزيافة عارضةٌ وعيب؛ لأنه غِش، فلا يحتمله مطلق الاسم، بل يكون رجوعاً، كدعوى الأجل في الدين، ودعوى الخيار في البيع.
And if he acknowledges dirhams as a loan or as the price of a sale, and says: 'They are counterfeit': it is valid according to them (the two) when connected, because dirhams are of two types: good and counterfeit, except that good ones are predominant, so the other becomes like a metaphor (majaz), thus the change to it is valid when connected. And Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is not accepted even if connected, because counterfeitness is an incidental defect and a flaw; it is cheating, so the absolute name does not bear it; rather it is a retraction, like claiming a term in a debt or claiming an option in a sale.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a person acknowledges a debt of dirhams as a loan or sale price, then states that the dirhams are counterfeit. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two), this is accepted as a modifying explanation (bayan mughayyar) if stated immediately (wasl), because dirhams can be good or counterfeit, and since good dirhams are the norm, calling them counterfeit is like a metaphorical specification. Abu Hanifa, however, rejects this, arguing that counterfeitness is an inherent defect and the general term 'dirhams' does not include counterfeit ones; thus, the statement constitutes a retraction, similar to claiming a deferred payment term or an option in a sale.

Key Terms
الزيوف (al-ziyuf): Counterfeit dirhams that have a defect or are fake.
المجاز (al-majaz): Metaphorical usage; here, referring to the less common type as if it were metaphorical because the common type is dominant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the term 'dirhams' is ambiguous.
B) Because counterfeitness is a defect that does not fit the general term.
C) Because the person is obviously lying.
D) Because the claim must be made before acknowledgment.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the general term 'dirhams' does not include incidental defects; only a predominant subtype can be specified metaphorically.

The Minor's Deposit: Exception or Null Act?
Dispute on whether a minor's deposit is an exception or a null act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an absolute act (fi'l mutlaq) and a general statement (amm) in Hanafi usul, and explain why Abu Hanifa and Muhammad reject treating a minor's deposit as an exception (istithna').

Core Idea
An absolute act cannot be broken by exception; only general statements admit istithna'.

This passage from Kanz al-Wusul applies a key usul principle: the distinction between absolute acts and general statements. The case of a minor's deposit illustrates how Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa/Muhammad disagree on whether the minor's specification of deposit counts as an exception from the authorization or as a conflicting claim that cannot stand. Understanding this distinction is crucial for parsing similar texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal act
The act being analysed is the minor's deposit (wadi'ah). What is the nature of the authorization (tasleet)? The passage discusses whether the authorization is a general statement or an absolute act.
2
Understand Abu Yusuf's argument
Abu Yusuf considers authorization as a general category with two subtypes: safekeeping and others. The specification of deposit is an exception (istithna') from that general category.
3
Understand Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's rebuttal
They argue that authorization is an absolute act (fi'l mutlaq), not a general statement. Exception can only apply to statements, not absolute acts. Therefore it becomes a mu'arada (conflicting claim) that cannot be validated.
Key phrase: 'التسليط فعل يوجد من المسلّط' (authorization is an act).
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Yusuf, the deposit is considered but ineffective due to lack of guardianship; under Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, it is entirely void.
5
Connect to Imam al-Shafi'i's view
The text notes that Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's view resembles al-Shafi'i's position on exception, highlighting cross-madhhab resonance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا الأصل: إيداع الصبي الذي يعقل. قال أبو يوسف رحمه الله : هو من باب الاستثناء؛ لأن إثبات اليد والتسليط نوعان للاستحفاظ، وغيره، فإذا نص على الإيداع كان مستثنى، والاستثناء من المتكلّم: تصرُّفٌ على نفسه، فلا يبطل ؛ لعدم الولاية، بل لا يثبت إلا الاستحفاظ ، ثم لا ينفذ الاستحفاظ ؛ لعدم الولاية على الصبي، فيصير كالمعدوم. وقال أبو حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله : ليس هذا من باب الاستثناء؛ لأن التسليط فعل يوجد من المسلّط، فلا يصح استثناء ما وراء الاستحفاظ منه، والفعل مطلق، لا عام، والمستثنى من خلاف جنسه، فيصير ذلك من باب المعارضة، فلا بد من تصحيحه شرعاً ليعارضه، ولم يوجد. وصار هذا مثل قول الشافعي رحمه الله في الاستثناء. وعلى هذا الأصل: قال أصحابنا رحمهم الله في كتاب الشركة في
And on this principle: the deposit of a minor who has understanding. Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is of the category of exception (istithna'), because establishing possession and authorization are two types for safekeeping and otherwise, so if he specifies deposit, it is excepted, and exception from the speaker is a disposition upon himself, so it is not invalid due to lack of guardianship; rather only safekeeping is established, then safekeeping is not executed due to lack of guardianship over the minor, so it becomes like non-existent. And Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said: This is not of the category of exception, because authorization is an act that proceeds from the authorizer, so exception of what is beyond safekeeping from it is not valid, and the act is absolute, not general, and the excepted item is of a different genus, so it becomes a matter of mu'arada (conflicting claims), and it must be legally validated to compete, but it is not found. And this became like the saying of al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) regarding exception. And on this principle: our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) said in the book of partnership...
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage applies the preceding principle of modifying explanations to the case of a minor (sabi) who has understanding (ya'qil) making a deposit (wadi'ah). Abu Yusuf considers the deposit as an exception (istithna') from the general act of authorization; the minor's statement specifying deposit is a disposition upon himself, so it is not invalid, but since the minor lacks legal authority, the safekeeping is not effective—it is as if non-existent. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that this is not an exception because authorization is an absolute act, not a general statement; exception cannot apply to an act. Instead, it is a case of conflicting claims (mu'arada), and since the act cannot be legally validated due to lack of capacity, it is void. They compare this to Imam al-Shafi'i's view on exception. The passage then starts to reference a similar principle in the book of partnership, but cuts off.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): An exception; a linguistic operation that excludes part of a general statement.
المعارضة (al-mu'arada): Conflicting claims or opposing considerations that cannot be reconciled.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Abu Hanifa and Muhammad reject treating a minor's deposit as an exception?
A) Because the minor lacks understanding.
B) Because the act of authorization is absolute, not general.
C) Because exception is not allowed in Shari'ah.
D) Because the deposit is a retraction.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, exception (istithna') applies only to general statements, not to absolute acts; therefore a minor's specification of deposit from an absolute authorization is invalid.

Exception vs Retraction in Sale Contract
Dispute over exception vs retraction in sale contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a valid exception (istithna') and a retraction (ruju') in a sale contract according to the Hanafi school, and explain the underlying usul principle regarding explicit vs. implicit obligations.

Core Idea
A statement that appears to be an exception may actually be a retraction if it contradicts an explicitly established obligation.

In this passage from Usul al-Bazdawi, we encounter a debate about the legal effect of the phrase 'except that I have not received it' in a sale contract. The question is whether this phrase qualifies as a valid exception that modifies the contract, or as a retraction that invalidates it. Abu Hanifa holds the latter view, grounding his argument in the principle that an explicitly established obligation cannot be overridden by an implicit implication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed statement
The phrase 'غير أني لم أقبضها' (except that I have not received it) is the contested utterance in the sale contract.
2
State the two opposing views
One view holds it is a clarifying exception (bayan mughayyir) and valid; Abu Hanifa considers it a retraction (ruju') and invalid.
3
Examine the reasoning for the first view
Non-receipt is a possible attribute of the sold item, so the exception clarifies without contradicting the original obligation.
This view sees the exception as highlighting a latent quality.
4
Examine Abu Hanifa's reasoning
The obligation of receipt is established explicitly (sarih); what is established by implication (dalala) cannot override it, so the later statement is a retraction.
Abu Hanifa prioritizes the explicit over the implicit.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
If valid exception, sale stands with modification; if retraction, sale is invalid.
This practical difference determines the contract's validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل في البيع وجوب المطالبة بالثمن، وقد يجب الثمن غير مطالب بأن يكون المبيعُ غير مقبوض ، فصار قوله : غير أني لم أقبضها مغيّراً للأصل، ولما كان كون المبيع غير مقبوض أحد محتمليه، لا من العوارض : كان بياناً مُغيّراً ، فصح موصولاً . ولأبي حنيفة رحمه الله : أن هذا رجوع وليس ببيان؛ لأن وجوب لا يُعرَف أثره [؟] دلالة قبضه [؟] والثابت بالدلالة [؟] مثله إذا الثمن مقابلاً بمبيع ثبت بالصريح، فإذا رجع لم يصح. وهذا فصل يطول شرحه.
The original principle in sale is the obligation to demand the price, and the price may become obligatory without being demanded because the sold item is not received. So his statement 'except that I have not received it' changes the original, and since the sold item not being received is one of its possibilities, not a defect, it is a clarifying change, so it is valid as connected exception. And according to Abu Hanifa, this is a retraction, not a clarification, because the obligation of [؟] is not known by indication, and what is established by indication is like [؟] if the price corresponds to a sold item established explicitly, then if he retracts it does not hold. This is a chapter whose explanation would be lengthy.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage opens a dispute about whether the phrase 'except that I have not received it' in a sale contract is a valid exception (istithna) or a retraction (ruju'). The view presented first holds that it is a clarification (bayan) because non-receipt is a possible attribute of the sold item, not a defect, so it modifies the original contract and is valid. Abu Hanifa disagrees, arguing that it is a retraction: the obligation to receive the price is known by explicit statement (sarih), and what is established by implication (dalala) cannot override what is explicit. Therefore, the later statement amounts to a retraction of the sale, which is invalid. The text notes this is a complex issue requiring lengthy explanation.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna'): An exception that excludes part of the subject from the ruling.
بيان (bayan): Clarification that modifies or explains the original statement.
رجوع (ruju'): Retraction, which annuls the earlier legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is a valid exception (istithna') that modifies the contract.
B) It is a retraction (ruju') that invalidates the sale.
C) It is a condition (shart) that renders the contract void.
D) It is an acknowledgment (iqrar) that has no effect.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a statement that implicitly contradicts an explicit obligation is treated as a retraction, not a valid exception.

Deposit by a Discerning Minor: Istithna vs Mu'arada
Dispute between Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa/Muhammad on deposit by a minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish istithna from mu'arada in the Hanafi usul framework and apply this distinction to the case of a discerning minor's deposit.

Core Idea
A discerning minor's deposit is not an exception but a mu'arada, because authorization is an absolute act, not a general statement.

In this lesson, we examine a classic usul dispute between Abu Yusuf and the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) concerning the legal effect of a deposit (wadi'ah) made by a discerning minor. The disagreement hinges on whether the minor's implicit restriction to safekeeping is an exception (istithna) from a general authorization or a conflicting condition (mu'arada) that vitiates the act. Understanding this distinction sharpens your ability to classify legal statements and their operative scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Characterization
First, note that the act in question is a discerning minor depositing an item with the intention of only safekeeping (istihfaz), not full authorization (taslit). The dispute is whether this stipulation is an exception or a conflict.
2
Analyze Abu Yusuf's Position: Exception
Abu Yusuf treats the minor's statement as an exception: the minor says 'I deposit for safekeeping,' which excludes other uses. He argues that an exception from the speaker is an act upon himself and does not require capacity for the excepted part. Thus, only safekeeping is established, though it fails due to lack of capacity.
This relies on treating the minor's utterance as a general statement that can be specified.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's Position: Mu'arada
They counter that authorization (taslit) is an absolute act (fi'l mutlaq), not a general statement (amm). Since the exception is of a different genus (it excludes authorization), it cannot be an exception; it becomes a conflicting condition (mu'arada). For a condition to conflict, it must be legally valid, but it is not, so the entire deposit is ineffective.
This emphasizes the usul principle: istithna requires a general statement; an absolute act cannot be excepted.
4
Compare the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
Under Abu Yusuf's view, the minor's intention of safekeeping is recognized but ineffective; under the majority view, the deposit as a whole is invalid because the conflicting condition prevents the contract from forming.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا الأصل: إيداع الصبي الذي يعقل. وقال أبو يوسف رحمه الله : هو من باب الاستثناء؛ لأن إثبات اليد والتسليط نوعان للاستحفاظ، وغيره، فإذا نص على الإيداع كان مستثنى، والاستثناء من المتكلّم: تصرُّفٌ على نفسه، فلا يبطل ؛ لعدم الولاية، بل لا يثبت إلا الاستحفاظ ، ثم لا ينفذ الاستحفاظ ؛ لعدم الولاية على الصبي، فيصير كالمعدوم. وقال أبو حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله : ليس هذا من باب الاستثناء؛ لأن التسليط فعل يوجد من المسلّط، فلا يصح استثناء ما وراء الاستحفاظ منه، والفعل مطلق، لا عام، والمستثنى من خلاف جنسه، فيصير ذلك من باب المعارضة، فلا بد من تصحيحه شرعاً ليعارضه، ولم يوجد.
And based on this principle: the deposit of a discerning minor. Abu Yusuf said: it is of the category of exception, because establishing hand and authorization are two types for safekeeping and other, so when he stipulates deposit it is excepted, and exception from the speaker is an act upon himself, so it does not become void due to lack of guardianship, rather only safekeeping is established, but the safekeeping is not executable due to lack of guardianship over the minor, so it becomes like non-existent. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad said: this is not of the category of exception, because authorization is an act that occurs from the authorizer, so excepting what is beyond safekeeping from him is not valid, and the act is absolute, not general, and the excepted item is of a different genus, so it becomes of the category of mu'arada (conflict), so it must be made valid legally to conflict, but it did not exist.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage applies the principle of exception to the case where a discerning minor entrusts a deposit (wadi'ah). Abu Yusuf treats the minor's statement as an exception: the minor intends only safekeeping, not full authorization, so only the safekeeping aspect is established. However, the safekeeping does not take effect due to the minor's lack of legal capacity (wilaya), rendering it void. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad disagree: they argue that the minor's action is an absolute act, not a general statement. Since the exception is of a different genus (it excludes authorization), it becomes a conflicting condition (mu'arada). For it to be effective, it would need to be legally validated, which it is not, so it fails. The difference hinges on whether the minor's statement is treated as a general declaration that can be specified, or an absolute act that cannot.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna'): An exception that excludes part of the meaning from a general statement.
مُعارَضَة (mu'arada): A conflicting condition that contradicts the core of the statement.
فعل مطلق (fi'l mutlaq): An absolute act, not containing a general or specific linguistic scope.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, why is a discerning minor's deposit not considered an exception?
A) Because the minor lacks legal capacity.
B) Because the minor's act is absolute, not general, and the exception is of a different genus.
C) Because the exception would require the minor to have ownership.
D) Because the deposit is invalid regardless.

Key Takeaway
A discerning minor's deposit is not an exception (istithna) but a conflicting condition (mu'arada) because authorization is an absolute act, not a general statement.

Exception vs Conflicting Condition in Sale Contracts
Ruling on two similar yet legally distinct expressions in sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between istithna' and shart mu'arid in a sale contract and apply the Hanafi ruling to similar expressions.

Core Idea
Exception removes part of the subject but not the price; a conflicting condition restructures the contract entirely.

In Hanafi fiqh, the precise wording of a contract can dramatically change the outcome. This passage examines two seemingly similar statements in a slave sale: 'I sold you this slave for 1000 dirhams except half of him' versus 'on condition that I have half of him'. While both result in half ownership, the price differs drastically. Understanding the linguistic and legal operation of each phrase is essential for correct application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two expressions
Read the two statements: 'except half' (istithna) and 'on condition that I have half' (shart mu'arid). Note that both modify the subject (the slave) but differ in form.
Istithna' uses 'illa' (except); shart mu'arid uses 'ala anna' (on condition that).
2
Analyze the effect of istithna'
In 'except half', the exception only removes half the slave from the sale, but the price remains unchanged. The seller is only selling what remains (half), but the full price is owed because the price is not affected by the exception.
Hanafi rule: al-istithna' yadkhulu 'ala al-mubayyi‘ la 'ala al-thaman.
3
Analyze the effect of shart mu'arid
In 'on condition that I have half', the condition conflicts with the initial offer (which implied full transfer). This condition forces a restructuring: the seller retains half ownership, so only half is sold, and the price is halved accordingly.
The condition is mu'arid (conflicting), so the contract becomes bilateral regarding ownership.
4
Examine the illustrative analogy
The text gives an example of buying two slaves where one already belongs to the buyer. The price is divided proportionally between the two slaves, similar to how the condition in the second statement forces division.
This analogy reinforces the idea of proportional division when ownership is shared.
5
Generalize the usul rule
From these cases, formulate the principle: in sale contracts, istithna' only affects the subject (mubayyi‘) and leaves the price intact, while a conflicting condition (shart mu'arid) restructures the contract, affecting both subject and price proportionally.
This rule applies to any sale where such expressions are used.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا الأصل: قال أصحابنا رحمهم الله في كتاب الشركة في رجل قال لآخر : بعتُ منك هذا العبد بألف درهم إلا نصفه : إن البيع يقع على النصف بألف. ولو قال: على أن لي نصفه : يقع على النصف بخمسمائة؛ لأن الاستثناء تكلّم بالباقي، وإنما دخل في المبيع، لا في الثمن، فيصير المبيع نصفاً، ويبقى كل الثمن. وقوله : على أن لي نصفه شرطٌ معارض لصدر الكلام، فيكون موجبه أن يعارض هذا الإيجاب الأول، فيصير العقد واقعاً للبائع والمشتري، فيصير بائعاً من نفسه ومن المشتري، والبيع من نفسه صحيح بحكمه إذا أفاد ، وفي الدخول فائدةً حكم التقسيم، فيصير داخلاً، خارجاً؛ ليخرج بقسطه من الثمن. ثم مثل مَن اشترى عبدين بألف درهم، أحدهما ملك المشتري : أن الثمن ينقسم عليهما ، ألا يُرى أن شراء مال المضاربة يصح بمباشرة رب المال.
And based on this principle: our companions said in the book of partnership about a man who said to another: 'I sold you this slave for one thousand dirhams except half of him': the sale takes place on half for [the full] thousand. And if he said: 'on condition that I have half of him': it takes place on half for five hundred. Because the exception speaks regarding the remainder, and it only enters into the sold item, not the price, so the sold item becomes half, and the entire price remains. And his statement 'on condition that I have half' is a condition conflicting with the initial statement, so its effect is that it confronts this first offer, so the contract becomes effective for the seller and the buyer, so the seller becomes a seller from himself and from the buyer, and the sale from himself is valid by its ruling if it benefits, and in entering there is a benefit of the ruling of division, so he becomes included, excluded to extract his portion of the price. Then an example: whoever buys two slaves for one thousand dirhams, one of them owned by the buyer: the price is divided upon them both. Do you not see that purchasing partnership capital is valid by the direct action of the owner of capital?
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on two similar but legally distinct expressions in a sale contract. When a seller says 'I sold you this slave for 1000 dirhams except half of him', the exception affects the subject (the slave) but not the price. Thus, the buyer receives half the slave but owes the full 1000 dirhams. This is because the exception (istithna) removes half from the subject without affecting the price. In contrast, if the seller says 'on condition that I have half of him', this is a condition (shart) that conflicts with the initial offer. It creates a mutual contract where the seller retains half ownership, effectively selling only half to the buyer but at half the price (500). This is because the condition forces a division of ownership and price. The text then gives an analogous example of buying two slaves where one already belongs to the buyer, and the price is divided proportionally. The principle is that exception and condition are treated differently: exception only affects the subject, while a conflicting condition restructures the contract.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna'): An exception that removes part of the subject (mubayyi‘) from the sale, but does not affect the price.
شرط معارض (shart mu’arid): A condition that conflicts with the core of the contract, requiring a restructuring.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A seller says: 'I sold you this slave for 1000 dirhams except half of him.' What is the buyer obligated to pay?
A) 500 dirhams for half the slave.
B) 1000 dirhams for half the slave.
C) 500 dirhams for the whole slave.
D) The sale is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sale contracts, istithna' only reduces the subject and keeps the price intact, while a conflicting condition (shart mu'arid) proportionally reduces both subject and price.

Agency with Condition Not to Confess: Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad
Dispute on the validity of a condition in litigation agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on the validity of a 'no-confession' condition in a litigation agency, and apply the concepts of majaz and dalalat al-diyana to similar usul disputes.

Core Idea
Confession is owned by the agent, not part of litigation; thus it cannot be excepted without revoking the agency — but custom may transform the metaphor.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul presents a classic Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on whether a condition prohibiting confession in a litigation agency is valid. The disagreement hinges on the nature of confession: is it an inherent part of litigation or a separate legal effect? The answer involves the usul concepts of haqiqa (literal meaning) and majaz (metaphorical extension), as well as the role of 'urf (custom) and dalalat al-diyana (religious indication).

Guided Path
1
State the legal issue
Identify the condition in the agency and what is being disputed: can the principal validly exclude the agent's power to confess?
2
Identify the two positions
Abu Yusuf holds the condition is void; Muhammad holds it is valid and the opponent can reject the agent.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's reasoning
Confession becomes owned by the agent through representation, not as part of litigation; therefore it cannot be excepted from the agency. Only revocation removes it.
Key distinction: confession is a separate legal effect, not subsumed under litigation.
4
Analyze Muhammad's reasoning
Litigation by custom includes confession via metaphorical usage (majaz). The condition transforms this metaphor into literal meaning (haqiqa) from a religious perspective (diyana), making the exception valid.
The transformation occurs through dalalat al-diyana.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If condition is void (Abu Yusuf), any confession by the agent binds the principal. If valid (Muhammad), the opponent can reject the agent, and the confession may not be binding.
Practical consequence in court.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا الأصل : رجل وكل وكيلاً بالخصومة، على أن لا يُقرَّ عليه، أو غير جائز الإقرار بطل هذا الشرط عند أبي يوسف رحمه الله ؛ لأَنَّ على قوله الإقرار يصير مملوكاً للوكيل؛ لقيامه مقام الموكل، لا لأنه من الخصومة، حتى لا يختص بمجلس الخصومة، فيصير ثابتاً بالوكالة حكماً، لا مقصوداً، فلا يصح استثناؤه، ولا إبطاله بالمعارضة، إلا بنقض الوكالة. وقال محمد رحمه الله : استثناؤه جائز، وللخصم أن لا يقبل هذا الوكيل؛ لأن الخصومة تناولت الإقرار؛ عملاً بمجازها، على ما عُرف، وانقلب المجاز ها هنا بدلالة الديانة حقيقةً وصارت الحقيقة كالمجاز.
And based on this principle: a man appointed an agent for litigation on condition that no confession be made against him, or that confession is impermissible; this condition is void according to Abu Yusuf, because according to him, confession becomes owned by the agent due to his standing in place of the principal, not because it is part of litigation, so it is not restricted to the litigation session, so it is established by agency as a legal effect, not as an intention, so its exception is not valid, nor its invalidation by conflict, except by revocation of the agency. And Muhammad said: its exception is permissible, and the opponent may reject this agent because litigation includes confession by way of its metaphorical usage (majaz), according to custom, and here the metaphorical becomes real (haqiqa) by the indication of religious obligation (diyana), and the real becomes like the metaphorical.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of a condition in a litigation agency that prohibits the agent from confessing. Abu Yusuf argues that the condition is void. His reasoning: confession is not inherently part of litigation (khosuma) but is a separate legal act that the agent owns by virtue of agency (niyaba). Therefore, it cannot be excepted from the agency; the only way to prevent it is to revoke the agency entirely. Muhammad disagrees: he considers the exception valid. He argues that litigation ordinarily includes confession as a metaphorical extension (majaz) based on custom ('urf). However, in this case, due to the condition, the metaphorical usage becomes literal (haqiqa) from a religious perspective (diyana), and the literal becomes metaphorical. Thus, the exception is allowed, and the opponent may reject this agent precisely because confession has been excluded. The difference stems from whether confession is considered a necessary part of litigation or a separate matter.

Key Terms
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical or extended meaning of a term based on custom.
دلالة الديانة (dalalat al-diyana): Indication based on religious obligation (interpreting actions in good faith).
حقيقة (haqiqa): Literal, original meaning of a word.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because confession is not part of agency at all.
B) Because confession becomes owned by the agent as a representative and cannot be excepted.
C) Because the condition violates public policy.
D) Because the agent cannot be bound by conditions.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a condition in litigation agency depends on whether confession is considered an inherent part of litigation or a separate legal effect; the Hanafi school has two views rooted in the usul concepts of majaz and dalalat al-diyana.

Exceptions and Conflicting Conditions: From Sale to Agency (Qiyas)
Applying istithna' and shart mu'arid from sale to agency via qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules of istithna' and shart mu'arid in contracts and extend them via qiyas to agency, and identify the preferred position in the intra-Hanafi dispute on excluding iqrar in wakala bil khusuma.

Core Idea
An exception reduces the subject matter; a conflicting condition transforms the contract. This principle applies to agency via qiyas.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Kanz al-Wusul establishes a key usul principle: how to distinguish between an exception (istithna') and a conflicting condition (shart mu'arid) in contracts. Using a sale example, the author extracts a rule and then extends it via qiyas to agency for litigation (wakala bil khusuma). The intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on excluding the agent's power of admission (iqrar) illustrates the practical impact of this qiyas. The preferred position (al-ashah) is Muhammad's view, supported by al-Khassaf.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the sale case: istithna' vs. shart mu'arid
Identify the two scenarios: 'I sold you this slave for 1000 dirhams except half of it' versus 'on condition that I have half of it.' Determine the ruling for each: the first is an exception reducing the sold item to half but keeping the full price; the second is a conflicting condition that transforms the contract into a sale of half for half the price (500 dirhams).
Exception affects the subject matter only; conflicting condition affects both parties' rights.
2
Extract the usul rule
Generalize: When a condition contradicts the initial offer's implication, the contract is reinterpreted, often resulting in a different price or ownership structure. The exception (istithna') is a valid exclusion from the subject matter; the conflicting condition (shart mu'arid) creates a new contractual relationship.
The rule is rooted in the linguistic analysis of the phrase and its legal effect.
3
Apply qiyas to wakala bil khusuma
The author applies the same principle to a case where a principal appoints an agent for litigation but excludes the power of admission (la yuqarru 'alayh). Abu Yusuf argues the condition is void because iqrar is a legal effect of the agency, not part of litigation. Muhammad counters that litigation metaphorically includes iqrar, so the condition is valid and restricts the agent's authority.
The qiyas hinges on whether iqrar is considered part of 'khusuma' (litigation) or an independent legal effect.
4
Evaluate the dispute and identify the preferred view
Muhammad's view is supported by al-Khassaf and deemed al-ashah (more sound). The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is that a principal can validly appoint an agent for litigation without granting authority to make admissions. This also applies to excluding denial (inkar).
The dispute is legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school; the tarjih follows linguistic and analogical reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجل قال لآخر : بعتُ منك هذا العبد بألف درهم إلا نصفه : إن البيع يقع على النصف بألف. ولو قال: على أن لي نصفه : يقع على النصف بخمسمائة؛ لأن الاستثناء تكلّم بالباقي، وإنما دخل في المبيع، لا في الثمن، فيصير المبيع نصفاً، ويبقى كل الثمن. : وقوله : على أن لي نصفه شرطٌ معارض لصدر الكلام، فيكون موجبه أن يعارض هذا الإيجاب الأول، فيصير العقد واقعاً للبائع والمشتري، فيصير بائعاً من نفسه ومن المشتري، والبيع من نفسه صحيح بحكمه إذا أفاد ، وفي الدخول فائدةً حكم التقسيم، فيصير داخلاً، خارجاً؛ ليخرج بقسطه من الثمن. ثم مثل مَن اشترى عبدين بألف درهم، أحدهما ملك المشتري : أن الثمن ينقسم عليهما ، ألا يُرى أن شراء مال المضاربة يصح بمباشرة رب المال. وعلى هذا الأصل : رجل وكل وكيلاً بالخصومة، على أن لا يُقرَّ عليه، أو غير جائز الإقرار بطل هذا الشرط عند أبي يوسف رحمه الله ؛ : لأن على قوله الإقرار يصير مملوكاً للوكيل ؛ لقيامه مقام الموكل، لا لأنه من الخصومة، حتى لا يختص بمجلس الخصومة، فيصير ثابتاً بالوكالة حكماً، لا مقصوداً، فلا يصح استثناؤه ، ولا إبطاله بالمعارضة، إلا بنقض الوكالة. 39 وقال محمد رحمه الله : استثناؤه جائز، وللخصم أن لا يقبل هذا الوكيل؛ لأن الخصومة تناولت الإقرار ؛ عملاً بمجازها، على ما عُرف، وانقلب المجاز ها هنا بدلالة الديانة حقيقةً وصارت الحقيقة كالمجاز، فإذا استثنى الإقرار، وقيد التوكيل كان بياناً مغيراً، فصح موصولاً . وعلى هذا يجب أن لا مفصولاً، إلا أن يعزله أصلاً؛ ولأنه عمل يصح م بحقيقة اللغة، فصح ، فلم يكن استثناء في الحقيقة. وعلى هذا يصح مفصولاً، وهو اختيار الخَصَّاف رحمه الله. واختلف في استثناء الإنكار، والأصح أنه على هذا الاختلاف، على الطريق الأول لمحمد رحمه الله.
A man said to another: I sold you this slave for a thousand dirhams except half of it: The sale takes place on half for a thousand. And if he said: on the condition that I have half of it: it takes place on half for five hundred; because the exception speaks of the remainder, and it only enters into the sold item, not into the price, so the sold item becomes half and all the price remains. And his saying: on the condition that I have half of it is a condition contradicting the beginning of the speech, so its implication is that it contradicts this first offer, so the contract becomes concluded for the seller and buyer, so the seller becomes a seller from himself and from the buyer, and the sale from himself is valid by its ruling if it benefits, and in entering there is a benefit of the rule of division, so he becomes entering and exiting so that he may take out his share of the price. Then an example: someone who buys two slaves for a thousand dirhams, one of them being owned by the buyer: the price is divided between them; do you not see that the purchase of mudaraba property is valid by direct action of the owner of capital. And on this principle: a man appointed an agent for litigation on the condition that no admission be made against him, or that admission is not permissible; this condition is void according to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him; because in his view the admission becomes owned by the agent due to his standing in place of the principal, not because it is part of litigation, so it is not confined to the litigation session, so it becomes established by the agency by legal effect, not by intent, so its exclusion is not valid, nor its cancellation by opposition, except by revoking the agency. And Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, said: its exclusion is valid, and the opponent may not accept this agent; because litigation includes admission; acting by its metaphorical sense, as is known, and the metaphor here turned into reality by the indication of religious duty, and reality became like metaphor; so when he excludes admission and restricts the appointment, it becomes a modified statement, so it is valid when connected. And on this it must not be severed unless he dismisses him completely; and because it is an action valid by the literal meaning of language, so it is valid, so it is not an exception in reality. And on this it is valid when severed, and this is the choice of al-Khassaf, may Allah have mercy on him. And there is disagreement regarding the exclusion of denial, and the most correct is that it follows this disagreement, on the first way of Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Kanz al-Wusul presents a usul principle about exceptions (istithna') and conflicting conditions (shart mu'arid) in contracts, using a sale case and then extending it via qiyas to agency (wakala). The first ruling: if someone says 'I sold you this slave for 1000 dirhams except half of it,' the sale is valid for half the slave at the full price (1000 dirhams). The exception pertains to the sold item, not the price, so the sold item is reduced to half but the price remains whole. The second ruling: if he says 'on condition that I have half of it,' this condition contradicts the initial offer. It transforms the contract such that the seller becomes a seller to himself as well, resulting in a sale of half the slave for half the price (500 dirhams). The author then applies the same principle via qiyas to agency for litigation: if a principal appoints an agent with the condition that the agent cannot make an admission (iqrar) against him, Abu Yusuf holds the condition void because the power of admission is inherent in the agency by legal effect, not by intention. Muhammad holds the condition valid because the agency can be restricted, and the exclusion of admission is a valid modification of the agent's authority. Al-Khassaf chooses Muhammad's view. The dispute also extends to the exclusion of denial (inkar). The relied-upon position (al-ashah) is according to Muhammad's first path. This illustrates how exceptions and conditions that conflict with the contract's essential nature are either invalid or cause reinterpretation of the contract.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna'): Exception: excluding part of the sold item from the contract while keeping the price whole.
شرط معارض (shart mu'arid): Conflicting condition: a condition that contradicts the original offer and transforms the contract's nature.
وكالة بالخصومة (wakala bi al-khusuma): Agency for litigation: appointing someone to represent the principal in court proceedings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The ruling on istithna' and shart mu'arid in sale applies via qiyas to agency, with the preferred view allowing restriction of the agent's power of admission.

Exception in Confession and Agency: A Modifying Explanation
Ruling on exception in iqrar and tawkeel as modifying explanation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an exception in iqrar/tawkeel constitutes a modifying explanation (bayan mughayyir) and apply the Hanafi rule that it is valid only when connected (mawsul) unless explicitly separated.

Core Idea
An exception in a confession is a modifying explanation, not a true exception—valid when connected, invalid when disconnected.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the act of making an exception (istithna) in a confession (iqrar) or agency (tawkeel) is treated as a modifying explanation (bayan mughayyir). This lesson examines the linguistic validity of such exceptions, the distinction between connected and disconnected exceptions, and the internal Hanafi disagreement on disconnected exceptions and the exception of denial (istithna al-inkar).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act: exception in iqrar/tawkeel
Recognize that the passage discusses cases where a person makes an exception or imposes a condition in a confession or agency document.
This is a linguistic act (istithna) applied to a legal statement.
2
Classify the linguistic operation as a modifying explanation
The exception is not a true exception (istithna haqiqi) but a 'modifying explanation' (bayan mughayyir) that alters the original statement.
This classification determines its validity conditions.
3
Apply the condition: connected vs. disconnected
The exception is valid only when uttered immediately after the statement (mawsul). If disconnected (mafsul), it is invalid unless the speaker explicitly separates (azl) the exception from the statement.
The textual rule: 'so it is valid when connected'.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on disconnected exception
Al-Khassaf held that it is also valid when disconnected, while the first path (Imam Muhammad) restricts validity to connected. The text leans towards the first path.
This disagreement is within the school.
5
Understand the exception of denial (istithna al-inkar)
There is a specific disagreement about whether an exception that negates the previous statement (e.g., 'You owe me nothing except...'?) is valid. The most correct view ties it to the earlier disagreement.
The text states it follows the same difference on the first path of Imam Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا استثنى الإقرار، وقيد التوكيل كان بياناً مغيراً، فصح موصولاً. وعلى هذا يجب أن لا مفصولاً، إلا أن يعزله أصلاً؛ ولأنه عمل يصح بحقيقة اللغة، فصح، فلم يكن استثناء في الحقيقة. وعلى هذا يصح مفصولاً، وهو اختيار الخَصَّاف رحمه الله. واختلف في استثناء الإنكار، والأصح أنه على هذا الاختلاف، على الطريق الأول لمحمد رحمه الله.
When he makes an exception in a confession and restricts an agency, it becomes a modifying explanation, so it is valid when connected. And similarly, it should not be disconnected unless he separates it completely. Because it is an act that is valid by the reality of language, so it is valid, and it is not an exception in reality. And similarly, it is valid when disconnected, and that is the choice of al-Khassaf (may Allah have mercy on him). And there is disagreement regarding the exception of denial, and the most correct is that it follows this same difference, according to the first way of Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him).
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage states a rule about istithna (exception) in iqrar (confession) and tawkeel (agency). The author explains that if a person makes an exception in a confession or places a condition in an agency, it is considered a modifying explanation (bayan mughayyir). Such an exception is valid when connected (mawsul) to the original statement. It should not be performed disconnectedly unless the speaker explicitly separates the exception from the main statement. The reasoning is linguistic: since the act of exception is valid according to the true nature of language, it remains valid, but it does not constitute a true exception (in the sense of istithna haqiqi) but rather an explanation. The passage then notes that al-Khassaf held the view that it is also valid when disconnected. Furthermore, there is a difference of opinion regarding the exception of denial (istithna al-inkar) – whether it is valid – and the soundest position is that it follows this same internal Hanafi divergence, according to the first path of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithnā’): An exception; a linguistic act that excludes part of a general statement.
إقرار (iqrār): Confession; admission of a right or fact.
توكيل (tawkīl): Agency; delegation of authority.
بيان مغير (bayān mughayyir): A modifying explanation that alters or specifies the original statement.
موصول (mawṣūl): Connected; the exception is uttered immediately after the statement.
مفصول (mafṣūl): Disconnected; the exception is uttered after a pause.
عزل (ʿazl): Separation; explicitly removing the exception from the statement.
استثناء الإنكار (istithnā’ al-inkār): Exception of denial; an exception that negates the previous statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is invalid because it is not a true exception.

D) It is invalid unless the speaker explicitly separates it.

Key Takeaway
An exception in iqrar/tawkeel is a modifying explanation (bayan mughayyir), valid when connected (mawsul) and invalid when disconnected (mafsul) unless explicitly separated.

Using Conjunction ('Atf) to Clarify Ambiguous Numbers: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Presents the Hanafi view that 'atf can disambiguate an amount, with Shafi'i opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi view on the use of 'atf to disambiguate a mujmal number and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using custom and linguistic indication.

Core Idea
In numbers, the conjunction 'waw' clarifies the type, not adds an independent item, due to custom and idafa-like structure.

When a debtor says 'I owe 100 and a dirham', is the debt 100 dirhams or 100 (unspecified) plus one dirham? This passage presents the Hanafi view that the conjunction clarifies the ambiguous amount, supported by customary omission and linguistic analogy. The Shafi'i school disagrees, insisting on explicit clarification. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to recognize how 'urf and dalalah operate in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the dispute
What is the legal scenario? Debtor says '100 and a dirham'. Hanafi vs Shafi'i on whether the conjunction clarifies the number.
The number 100 is mujmal (ambiguous) because it lacks a specified type.
2
State the Hanafi position and its two proofs
Hanafis rule the debt is 100 dirhams. Proof 1: Customary omission of the head noun in numbers. Proof 2: The conjunction functions like idafa, where the second element defines the first.
3
State the Shafi'i position and its proof
Shafi'is rule the hundred is ambiguous; the debtor owes 100 (unspecified) plus one dirham. Proof: The number is mujmal, and conjunction is not placed for bayan.
They cite examples like '100 and a garment' where custom does not support clarification.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to Shafi'i
Hanafis respond that custom ('adah) is well-established for numbers due to frequency, and linguistically the conjoined pair acts as a single unit, analogous to idafa.
The key is the distinction between quantified numbers and generic items.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis rule the debtor owes 100 dirhams; Shafi'is rule he owes 100 plus one dirham separately.
This affects debt collection and contract interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث : فمثل سكوت المولى حين يرى عبده يبيع ويشتري : فيُجعل إذناً؛ دفعاً للغرور عن الناس. وكذلك سكوت الشفيع : جعل رداً؛ لهذا المعنى. وأما الرابع فمثل قول علمائنا رحمهم الله : في رجل قال: لفلان عليَّ مائةٌ ودينار، أو مائةٌ ودرهم: إن العطف جعل بياناً للأول، وجعل من جنس المعطوف. وكذلك : لفلان علي مائة وقفيز حنطة. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : القول قوله في المائة؛ لأنها مجملة، فإليه بيانها، والعطف لا يصلح بياناً ؛ لأنه لم يوضع له له. كما إذا قال : مائة وثوب، أو : مائة وشاة، أو : مائة وعبد. ووجه قولنا : أن هذا جعل بياناً عادةً، ودلالة. أما العادة: فلأن حذف المعطوف عليه في العدد متعارف ضرورة؛ لكثرة العدد، وطول الكلام يقول الرجل: بعتُ منك هذا العبد بمائة وعشرة دراهم، وبمائة وعشرين درهماً، وبمائة ودرهم ودرهمين: على السواء. وليس كذلك حكم ما هو غير مقدَّرٍ ؛ لأنه لا يثبت دَيْناً في الذمة ثبوت الأول. وأما الدلالة : فلأن المعطوف مع المعطوف عليه : بمنزلة شيء واحد، كالمضاف مع المضاف إليه، والمضاف إليه للتعريف، فإذا صلح العطف للتعريف : صح الحذف في المضاف إليه بدلالة العطف.
As for the third [type of silence]: it is like the silence of the master when he sees his slave buying and selling: it is made permission; to prevent deception of people. Similarly, the silence of the one entitled to pre-emption is made a rejection; for this meaning. As for the fourth [type]: it is like the statement of our scholars, may Allah have mercy on them: concerning a man who said: 'So-and-so has upon me a hundred and a dinar, or a hundred and a dirham': that the conjunction is made a clarification of the first, and is made of the same type as the conjoined item. Also: 'So-and-so has upon me a hundred and a qafiz of wheat.' And al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The statement is his word concerning the hundred, because it is ambiguous, and its clarification is up to him, and conjunction is not valid as a clarification because it was not placed for that. Such as when he says: 'a hundred and a garment,' or 'a hundred and a sheep,' or 'a hundred and a slave.' And the justification of our view: that this is made a clarification by custom and by indication. As for custom: because the omission of the conjoined item in numbers is conventionally known out of necessity, due to frequency of numbers and length of speech. A man says: 'I sold you this slave for a hundred and ten dirhams, and for a hundred and twenty dirhams, and for a hundred and one dirham and two dirhams'—all the same. And the ruling is not so for what is not quantified; because it does not establish a debt in the liability with the establishment of the first. As for indication: because the conjoined and the conjoined-to are like a single thing, like the construct state with its annex, and the annex is for definition, so if conjunction is valid for definition, then the omission in the annex is valid by the indication of conjunction.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage continues al-Bazdawi's enumeration of types of silence. The third type is the master's silence when seeing his slave transact—this silence is deemed permission to prevent public deception. Similarly, the silence of the person entitled to pre-emption (shafi') is treated as a rejection of the right. The fourth type is a distinct usul matter: when someone says 'I owe 100 and a dinar' or '100 and a dirham', our Hanafi scholars rule that the conjunction (waw) clarifies the ambiguous '100', making it 100 of the same type as the conjoined item. So '100 and a dirham' means 100 dirhams. The same applies to '100 and a qafiz of wheat' meaning 100 qafiz. Al-Shafi'i disagrees: the 100 is ambiguous (mujmal), so it is up to the speaker to clarify; the conjunction cannot clarify because it is not semantically designed for that purpose, similar to saying '100 and a garment' which leaves both uncertain. The Hanafi response rests on two bases: (1) Customarily, people commonly omit the 'ma'tuf alayh' (the head noun) in numbers due to frequency and brevity, e.g., saying 'I sold for 100 and ten dirhams' meaning 110 dirhams. This is universally accepted. (2) Linguistically, the conjoined and its head are treated as a unit, like the construct phrase (idafa). Since the annexed term serves to define, the conjunction can also define by indication, justifying the omission of the head. The Hanafis distinguish this from non-quantified items (like '100 and a garment') where custom does not support such interpretation.

Key Terms
عطف ('atf): Grammatical conjunction, usually with waw, used to join two items.
مجمل (mujmal): A term requiring external clarification to determine its specific meaning.
عادة ('adah): Customary practice recognized as a legal indicator.
دلالة (dalalah): Indication or inference from linguistic structure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis allow 'atf to clarify an ambiguous number like '100 and a dirham'?
A) Because the waw is grammatically designed for clarification.
B) Because customarily people omit the head noun in numbers and the conjunction functions like idafa.
C) Because al-Shafi'i agreed with them on this point.
D) Because the number 100 is inherently clear.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the conjunction ('atf) clarifies an ambiguous number through established custom and linguistic indication, while Shafi'i requires explicit bayan.

Conjunction as Clarification of Ambiguous Numbers: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Analysis of 'atf as bayan for mujmal numbers with madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a conjuncted term (atf) can serve as a bayan (clarification) for a preceding mujmal number when the conjuncted item is quantifiable (muqaddar).

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, a conjunction can clarify an ambiguous number when the conjuncted item is a standard measure.

This lesson explores a specific linguistic-legal dispute: when someone says 'I owe one hundred and a dirham,' does the conjunction 'and a dirham' clarify that the hundred refers to dirhams? The Hanafi school says yes, based on custom and linguistic analogy. Al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that conjunction cannot clarify ambiguity. Understanding this shows how Hanafi usul integrates custom (urf) and linguistic structure (idafah analogy) to resolve textual ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed case
The case is an iqrar (confession) where someone says 'I owe one hundred and a dirham' (or similar). The issue is whether the hundred is clarified as dirhams by the conjuncted term.
This is the fourth type of bayan discussed in the passage.
2
State the Hanafi position and its proofs
The Hanafi position is that the conjunction serves as bayan making the hundred dirhams. The proofs are: (1) custom (urf) of omitting the unit in numbers, and (2) linguistic indication that the conjuncted pair functions like an idafah construction, where the second term specifies the first.
Quantifiable items (muqaddarat) like dirhams and qafiz are treated differently from non-quantifiables.
3
State the Shafi'i position and its proof
Al-Shafi'i holds that the hundred remains ambiguous because the conjunction was not placed for clarification; it is merely additive. He analogizes to non-quantifiable items like garments.
The Shafi'i view treats all numbers alike when followed by a conjuncted noun.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to Shafi'i
The Hanafi response distinguishes muqaddarat from ghayr muqaddarat. In muqaddarat, custom supports the omission, and the conjunction operates like idafah. In non-quantifiables, the conjunction does not achieve specification.
The key distinction is whether the conjuncted item is a standard measure or not.
5
Apply the ruling to similar cases
The passage also covers agreed cases (twenty-one dirhams, one hundred and three dirhams) and the unique view of Abu Yusuf extending the principle to non-quantifiables like garments, except for slaves.
Note the consensus on certain forms and the intra-Hanafi variation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرابع فمثل قول علمائنا رحمهم الله : في رجل قال: لفلان عليَّ مائةٌ ودينار، أو مائةٌ ودرهم: إن العطف جعل بياناً للأول، وجعل من جنس المعطوف. 1 وكذلك : لفلان علي مائة وقفيز حنطة. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : القول قوله في المائة؛ لأنها مجملة، فإليه بيانها، والعطف لا يصلح بياناً ؛ لأنه لم يوضع له. كما إذا قال : مائة وثوب، أو : مائة وشاة، أو : مائة وعبد. ووجه قولنا : أن هذا جعل بياناً عادةً، ودلالة. أما العادة: فلأن حذف المعطوف عليه في العدد متعارف ضرورة؛ لكثرة العدد، وطول الكلام. يقول الرجل: بعتُ منك هذا العبد بمائة وعشرة دراهم، وبمائة وعشرين درهماً، وبمائة ودرهم ودرهمين: على السواء. الأول. وليس كذلك حكم ما هو غير مقدَّرٍ ؛ لأنه لا يثبت دَيْناً في الذمة ثبوت. وأما الدلالة : فلأن المعطوف مع المعطوف عليه : بمنزلة شيء واحد، كالمضاف مع المضاف إليه، والمضاف إليه للتعريف، فإذا صلح العطف للتعريف : صح الحذف في المضاف إليه بدلالة العطف. والعطف إذا كان في المقدرات صلح للتعريف، فجعل دليلاً على المضاف إليه. وإذا لم يكن مقدَّراً مثلُ الثوب، والفرس: لم يصلح للتعريف، فلم يصلح دليلا على المحذوف. كله واتفقوا في قول الرجل : لفلان علي أحد وعشرون درهماً: أن ذلك دراهم؛ لأن العشرين مع الآحاد معدود مجهول، فصح التعريف : بالدرهم. وكذلك إذا قال : أحد وعشرون شاةً، أو ثوباً. وأجمعوا في قوله : لفلان علي مائة وثلاثة دراهم فصاعداً: أن المائة من الدراهم؛ لأن الجملتين جميعاً أضيفتا إلى الدراهم، فصار بياناً لهما. وكذلك إذا قال : مائة وثلاثة أثواب، أو ثلاث شياه. في قوله: لفلان عليَّ مائة وثوب، أو: مائةٌ وشاة : إنه يُجعل بياناً ؛ لأن العطف دليل الاتحاد، مثلُ الإضافة، فكل جملة تحتمل القسمة فإنها تحتمل الاتحاد، فلذلك جعل بياناً، بخلاف قوله : مائة وعبد، والله أعلم.
As for the fourth type: it is like the statement of our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) concerning a man who said: 'So-and-so has one hundred and a dinar owed to me,' or 'one hundred and a dirham': that the conjunction is made a clarification (bayan) for the first [term], and it is made [i.e., the hundred] to be of the same type as the conjuncted [term]. And similarly: 'So-and-so has one hundred and a qafiz of wheat.' Al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'His word is taken [to mean only] the hundred, because it is ambiguous (mujmal), and its clarification is up to him, and conjunction is not valid as a clarification because it was not placed for that. [It is] just as if he said: one hundred and a garment, or one hundred and a sheep, or one hundred and a slave.' The proof of our statement is that this is made a clarification by custom (adah) and by indication (dalalah). As for custom: because omitting the conjuncted-upon [term] in numbers is conventionally known out of necessity, due to the frequency of numbers and the length of speech. A man says: 'I sold you this slave for one hundred and ten dirhams, and for one hundred and twenty dirhams, and for one hundred and a dirham and two dirhams' — all equally. The first. But the ruling of that which is not quantifiable (muqaddar) is not like that, because it does not establish a debt in the dhimmah as [a quantifiable] does. As for indication: because the conjuncted term together with the conjuncted-upon term are like a single thing, like the mudaf with the mudaf ilayh, and the mudaf ilayh is for specification (ta'rif). So if the conjunction is valid for specification, then the omission in the mudaf ilayh is valid by the indication of the conjunction. And conjunction, when it is in quantifiables, is valid for specification, so it is made a proof for the mudaf ilayh. But when it is not quantifiable, like a garment or a horse, it is not valid for specification, so it is not a valid proof for the omitted [term]. All of that. And they agreed concerning the statement 'So-and-so has twenty-one dirhams owed to me': that those are dirhams, because the twenty with the units is a numbered unknown, so specification by 'dirham' is valid. Similarly if he said: 'twenty-one sheep' or 'garments.' And they (the scholars) unanimously agreed concerning his statement 'So-and-so has one hundred and three dirhams and upwards': that the hundred is of dirhams, because both phrases are added to dirhams, so it becomes a clarification for both. Similarly if he said: 'one hundred and three garments' or 'three sheep.' And Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) said concerning his statement 'So-and-so has one hundred and a garment, or one hundred and a sheep': that it is made a clarification, because conjunction is a proof of unity (ittihad), like idafah. So every phrase that admits division also admits unity, therefore it is made a clarification, unlike his statement 'one hundred and a slave.' And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the fourth type of bayan in Usul al-Bazdawi. The author discusses a specific linguistic-legal case: when someone makes an iqrar (confession) saying 'I owe so-and-so one hundred and a dinar' or 'one hundred and a dirham.' The Hanafi position, stated by 'our scholars' (i.e., Hanafi fuqaha), is that the conjunction (al-'atf) makes the hundred clarified as being of the same type as the conjuncted item. For example, 'one hundred and a dirham' means one hundred dirhams. Similarly, 'one hundred and a qafiz of wheat' means one hundred qafiz of wheat. Al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that the word 'hundred' is ambiguous (mujmal) and the conjunction cannot serve as a clarification (bayan) because 'atf was not legislated for that purpose. He analogizes it to saying 'one hundred and a garment' where no such clarification is made. The Hanafi response provides two reasons: (1) Custom (urf): In everyday speech, when numbers are involved, people commonly omit the unit (e.g., 'one hundred and ten' meaning dirhams) due to frequency and brevity. This is different from non-quantifiable items. (2) Linguistic indication (dalalah): The conjuncted term together with its antecedent function like an idafah (genitive construction), where the second term specifies the first. In quantifiable items (like dirhams, which are standard monetary units), the conjunction effectively specifies. For non-quantifiable items (e.g., a garment, a horse), the conjunction does not serve as a valid specification. The passage then notes consensus on cases like 'twenty-one dirhams' (where the number clearly requires a specified unit) and 'one hundred and three dirhams' (where both phrases are explicitly added to 'dirhams'). Finally, a unique view of Imam Abu Yusuf is mentioned: he extends the principle to even 'one hundred and a garment' or 'one hundred and a sheep,' because conjunction indicates unity, similar to idafah; but he distinguishes 'one hundred and a slave' possibly because a slave is not a standard unit of measurement. The overall ruling is that in Hanafi fiqh, when a number is followed by a conjuncted term that is a standardized measure or currency, the conjunction clarifies the number's type.

Key Terms
العطف (al-'atf): A conjunction (usually 'wa') linking two nouns, where in this context it is argued to serve as a clarifier (bayan) for the first noun.
البيان (al-bayan): Clarification of an ambiguous term; here, the conjuncted term is said to specify the type of the preceding number.
المقدرات (al-muqaddarat): Quantifiable items that serve as standard measures or currency (e.g., dirhams, dinars, qafiz), which can be the subject of custom in omission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The hundred is ambiguous, and the debtor must clarify what type it is.
B) The hundred is clarified as dirhams because the conjunction 'and a dirham' serves as a bayan.
C) The statement is invalid because it is contradictory.
D) It is treated as one hundred dirhams only if the debtor intended it that way.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a conjunction can serve as a clarification (bayan) for an ambiguous number when the conjuncted item is a quantifiable (muqaddar) like dirhams, based on custom and linguistic analogy.
