Effect of Restriction (Qayd) on Rulings: The Independent Basis Principle
Explaining the independent legal effect of a restriction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a restriction (qayd) creates an independent legal basis and derive rulings in illustrative furu' cases.

Core Idea
A restriction is not a mere specification; it is the source of a new ruling.

This passage delves into a foundational Hanafi usul principle: when the Lawgiver adds a restriction to a general text, that restriction does not simply clarify the original command. Instead, it establishes its own ruling. We will analyze several case studies—including the obligation of reciting al-Fatiha, purity in tawaf, and the status of small amounts of intoxicants—to see how this principle operates and its implications for fiqh derivation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
From the opening sentence: a restriction (qayd) such as 'faith' does not derive its ruling from the original general text but from the restriction itself. Understand that the restriction is the legal basis, not the original command.
This contrasts with views that treat restrictions as mere exceptions.
2
Analyze the first two examples: Fatiha and purity in tawaf
The author states recitation of al-Fatiha is not obligatory (in Hanafi view) and purity is not a condition for tawaf because both would be additions (ziyada) that, if required, would change the ruling. Recognize that these restrictions are independent additions, not derived from the original general commands.
These examples highlight the practical fiqh differences between madhahib.
3
Examine the intoxicant and water examples
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule that a small amount of a triple-intoxicant is not prohibited because it is only part of the cause (the intoxicant), and a part does not take the ruling of the whole. Similarly, a small amount of water is not a complete purifier. Understand that the restriction (small amount) creates its own ruling—non-prohibition or non-purification.
This shows the principle applied to quantitative restrictions.
4
Understand the comparison to abrogation
The author compares restriction to abrogation (naskh): just as abrogation opposes an existing ruling, a restriction opposes the absolute (mutlaq) text. See restriction as a form of partial naskh in terms of effect.
This reinforces that restriction is not mere interpretation.
5
Apply the principle to the testimony analogy
If witnesses disagree on the price, the sale is not established. This is because the increase over the price makes the original price a part, and a part has no legal existence. The restriction (agreement on price) is the basis, and its failure voids the sale.
This analogy demonstrates the principle in muamalat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت قيد الإيمان لم تكن المؤمنة ثابتة بذلك النص الأول بنظمه، بل بهذا القيد، فيكون للإثبات ابتداءً، ودليل الخصوص: للإخراج، لا للإثبات.
ولا يُشكل أن النفي إذا ألحق بالجلد لم يبق الجلد حداً، ولهذا لم نجعل قراءة الفاتحة فرضاً؛ لأنها زيادة، ولم نجعل الطهارة في الطواف شرطاً؛ لأنها زيادة. ولهذا قال أبو حنيفة وأبو يوسف رحمهما الله: إن القليل من المثلث لا يَحْرُمُ بِوَجْهٍ؛ لأنه بعضُ المُسْكِر، وليس لبعض العلة حكم العلة. وكذلك الجنب والمحدث لا يستعملان الماء القليل عندنا؛ لأنه بعض المطهر، فلم يكن مطهراً كاملاً. ولأن دليل النسخ ما لو جاء مقارناً كان معارضاً، والقيد يعارض الإطلاق، بمنزلة سائر وجوه النسخ.
ونظير هذا الأصل: اختلاف الشهود في قدر الثمن أن البيع لا يثبت؛ لأن الزيادة على الثمن تجعل الأول بعضه، وقد صار كلاً من وجه، فصارا غيرين، ولم يكن للبعض حكم الوجود، والله أعلم.
And when the restriction of faith is established, the believing woman is not established by that first text in its wording, but rather by this restriction, so it is for affirmation initially, and the evidence of specificity is for exclusion, not for affirmation. And it is not problematic that negation, if attached to flogging, would not leave flogging as a hadd penalty, and for this we did not make recitation of al-Fatiha obligatory because it is an addition, and we did not make purity in tawaf a condition because it is an addition. And for this Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on them, said: 'A small amount of the triple-intoxicant is not prohibited at all; because it is part of the intoxicant, and a part of the cause does not have the ruling of the cause.' Similarly, the junub and the one with minor impurity do not use a small amount of water according to us; because it is part of the purifier, so it is not a complete purifier. And because the evidence of abrogation, if it came concurrently, would be contradictory, and the restriction opposes the absolute, akin to other forms of abrogation. And an analogy to this principle: the disagreement of witnesses regarding the amount of the price: that the sale is not established; because the increase over the price makes the first part of it, and it has become a whole from one aspect, so they become two different things, and a part does not have the ruling of existence. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of a legal principle in usul al-fiqh concerning the effect of adding a restriction (qayd) to a general text. The author argues that when a restriction is introduced, the ruling does not derive from the original general text but from the restricted expression itself. Thus, the restriction serves as an independent basis for the ruling, not merely a specification that excludes individuals. The author provides several examples to illustrate the point: 1) Recitation of al-Fatiha in prayer is not made obligatory by the general command to recite, because the addition of a condition (the sequence) would change the ruling; 2) Purification (tahara) as a condition for tawaf is not established from the general command because it would be an addition; 3) Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that a small amount of an intoxicant (al-muthallath) is not prohibited because it is only part of the intoxicant, and the ruling of the cause does not apply to a part; 4) A person in major or minor impurity may not use a small quantity of water because it is only part of the purifier and not a complete purifier. The author then compares restriction to abrogation: if evidence of abrogation came concurrently, it would conflict, and similarly restriction conflicts with absolute expression. Finally, an analogy is drawn from the law of testimony: if witnesses disagree on the price, the sale is not established because the increase makes the first price a part, and a part has no legal effect.

Key Terms
قيد (qayd): A restriction or condition added to a general text that specifies its application.
زيادة (ziyada): An addition to the original text that changes the ruling if it were not present.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is not intoxicating.
B) Because a part of the cause does not take the ruling of the cause.
C) Because it is permitted by a specific text.
D) Because it is analogous to water.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that a restriction creates an independent legal basis is essential for understanding why additions to general texts often result in distinct rulings rather than mere specifications.

Categories of Prophetic Actions and the Dispute on Followership
Analyzing categories of Prophetic acts and scholarly dispute on following them

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three main positions on whether non-obligatory Prophetic actions are binding, and evaluate which is mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school holds that Prophetic actions default to permissibility, but following is obligatory when evidence of general application exists.

The Prophet's actions are a primary source of law, but not all are equally binding. Hanafi usul scholars debated whether every act of the Prophet (other than the slip) must be followed. This lesson maps the three positions—waqf, mutaba'ah, and the nuanced Hanafi view—and explains why al-Jassas's position became the relied-upon view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Categories
The passage lists four main categories of Prophetic actions (mubah, mustahabb, wajib, fard) and excludes 'az-zallah' (the slip). Note that zallah is not a basis for emulation.
Zallah is an unintentional forbidden act resulting from a slip, distinct from deliberate sin (ma'siyah).
2
State the Three Positions
Identify the three positions: (1) Waqf – suspend judgment until evidence clarifies the ruling; (2) Mutaba'ah – obligatory to follow all actions; (3) Hanafi split – al-Karkhi (default ibaha) and al-Jassas (default obligation to follow unless proven specific).
The author sides with al-Jassas: 'This is more correct according to us.'
3
Examine the Proofs
Analyze the Quranic evidence cited by the mutaba'ah group (24:63) and the waqf group's reasoning (imitation requires knowledge of description). The Hanafi response uses certainty (yaqin) and the existence of Prophet-specific acts (ikhtisas).
Al-Karkhi and al-Jassas both rely on yaqin but differ on the default.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Tarjih
Explain why al-Jassas's view is preferred: certainty of obligation to follow is established by general commands, but can be qualified by evidence of specificity. This balances the apparent general command with actual Prophetic practice.
The tarjih is based on the principle that certainty remains until evidence shifts it.
5
Determine Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: If a mujtahid sees the Prophet performing an act of unknown status, al-Jassas says to follow it unless evidence of specificity emerges; al-Karkhi says it is permissible but not required; the waqf group says to abstain until the ruling is clear.
This affects how new sunnah practices are inferred from reported Prophetic actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب أفعال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم
وهي أربعة أقسام: مباح، ومستحب، وواجب، وفرض
وفيها قسم آخر، وهو الزَّلَّة، لكنه ليس من هذا الباب في شيء؛ لأنه لا يصلح للاقتداء، ولا يخلو عن بيان مقرون به من جهة الفاعل، أو من الله تبارك وتعالى، كما قال جل وعزّ وَعَصَى ءَادَمُ رَبَّهُ فَغَوى طه / ۱۲۱. وقال جل وعزَّ حكايةً عن موسى عليه السلام في قتل القبطي : ﴿قَالَ هَذَا مِنْ عَمَلِ الشَّيْطَنِ القصص / ١٥.
والزَّلَّة : اسمٌ لفعل غير مقصود في عينه، لكنه اتصل الفاعل به عن فعل مباح قَصَدَه ، فزَل بشغله عنه إلى ما هو حرام لم يقصده أصلاً. بخلاف المعصية : فإنها اسم لفعل حرام مقصود بعينه.
* واختلفوا في سائر أفعال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مما ليس بسهو، ولا طَبْع ؛ لأن البشر لا يخلو عما جبل عليه :
فقال بعضهم : يجب الوقفُ فيها.
وقال بعضهم: بل يلزمنا اتباعه فيها.
وقال الكرخي رحمه الله : نعتقد فيها الإباحة، فلا تُثبت الفضل إلا بدليل، ولا تُثبت المتابعة منا إياه فيها إلا بدليل.
وقال الجصاص رحمه الله مثل قول الكرخي، إلا أنه قال: علينا اتباعه، لا نترك ذلك إلا بدليل.
وهذا أصح عندنا.
وأما الواقفون فقد قالوا: إن صفة الفعل إذا كانت مشكلة: امتنع الاقتداء به؛ لأن الاقتداء هو المتابعة في أصله ووصفه، فإذا خالفه في الوصف : لم يكن مقتدياً، فوجب الوقف إلى أن يظهر.
وأما الآخرون فقد احتجوا بالنص الموجب لطاعة الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام قال الله تعالى: ﴿ فَلْيَحْذَرِ الَّذِينَ يُخَالِفُونَ عَنْ أَمْرِ ا النور / ٦٣ ، والنصوص في ذلك كثيرة.
وأما الكرخي الثابتة بيقين فلم يجز إثبات غيرها إلا بدليل، ووجب إثبات اليقين، كمن وكل رجلاً بماله يثبتُ الحفظ به؛ لأنه يقين، وقد وجدنا اختصاص الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ببعض ما فَعَلَه ، ووجدنا الاشتراك أيضاً.
Chapter: The Actions of the Prophet (peace be upon him). They are four categories: permissible, recommended, obligatory, and compulsory. There is another category, the 'slip', but it is not part of this chapter at all; because it is not suitable for following, and it is never without an accompanying explanation from the doer's side or from Allah Almighty, as He said: 'And Adam disobeyed his Lord and erred' (Taha 121). And Allah Almighty said narrating about Moses (peace be upon him) regarding the killing of the Copt: 'He said: This is of the work of Satan' (Al-Qasas 15). The 'slip' is a name for an act not intended in itself, but the doer was connected to it through a permissible act he intended, and he slipped from it to what is forbidden without originally intending it. Unlike disobedience: it is a name for a forbidden act intended in itself. And they differed regarding the rest of the actions of the Prophet (peace be upon him) that are not forgetfulness nor inherent nature; because humans are not free from what they are disposed to. Some said: stopping (waqf) is obligatory regarding them. Others said: rather, following him is incumbent upon us. Al-Karkhi (may Allah have mercy on him) said: we consider them permissible; so merit is not established except by evidence, nor is our following him in them established except by evidence. Al-Jassas (may Allah have mercy on him) said similar to al-Karkhi's statement, except that he said: it is upon us to follow him; we do not abandon that except by evidence. This is more correct according to us. As for those who stop (waqf), they said: if the description of the act is problematic, imitation of him is prevented; because imitation is following in its essence and description; if one differs in description, one is not imitating, so stopping is necessary until it becomes clear. As for the others, they argued with the text that necessitates obedience to the Messenger (peace and blessings be upon him). Allah Almighty said: 'So let those who oppose his command beware' (An-Nur 63), and the texts on that are many. As for al-Karkhi, what is established with certainty, it is not permissible to establish other than it except by evidence, and certainty must be established, like one who appoints a man over his wealth; preservation is established by him because it is certain. And we have found the Prophet (peace be upon him) being singled out for some of what he did, and we have also found commonality.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and dispute from the Hanafi usul tradition. The author begins by categorizing the actions of the Prophet (peace be upon him) into four types: mubah (permissible), mustahabb (recommended), wajib (obligatory), and fard (compulsory). He then introduces a fifth category, 'az-zallah' (the slip), which is an unintentional error that occurs when one intends a permissible act but inadvertently falls into something forbidden. This is distinct from 'ma'siyah' (disobedience), which is a deliberate sinful act. The 'zallah' is not included in the chapter because it is not a valid basis for emulation. The author then presents a disagreement among scholars regarding the Prophet's actions that are neither forgetfulness nor part of his inherent human nature. Three positions are given: (1) Some say we must suspend judgment (waqf) and not follow until the ruling is clear. (2) Others say we must follow him in all such actions. (3) Al-Karkhi holds that the default is permissibility (ibaha); neither special merit nor obligation to follow is established without evidence. Al-Jassas agrees with al-Karkhi but adds that following is obligatory unless evidence shows otherwise, and this is considered the most correct view in the Hanafi school. The author then elaborates the arguments: the 'waqf' group argues that if the legal description of an act is unknown, imitation is impossible because it requires matching both essence and description. The 'mutaba'ah' group relies on general Quranic commands to obey the Prophet, such as 24:63. Al-Karkhi's reasoning is based on certainty: what is known with certainty (like permissibility) cannot be overturned without evidence, similar to a trustee who is presumed trustworthy unless proven otherwise. The passage concludes by noting that the Prophet was singled out for some actions (e.g., more than four wives) while others are shared with the ummah, so generalizing requires evidence.

Key Terms
الزَّلَّة (az-zallah): An unintentional slip by the Prophet into a forbidden act while intending a permissible one, which is not a basis for emulation.
الإباحة (al-ibaha): Default legal permissibility; an action is permitted without reward or punishment unless evidence indicates otherwise.
الوقف (al-waqf): Suspending judgment on a legal ruling until evidence clarifies the matter; not to follow or prohibit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author of this passage, which position is considered the most correct?
A) The waqf position (suspending judgment).
B) The mutaba'ah position (obligation to follow all actions).
C) Al-Karkhi's position (default ibaha, no following without evidence).
D) Al-Jassas's position (default obligation to follow unless evidence of specificity).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school adopts al-Jassas's position: Prophetic actions are presumptively binding unless evidence shows they were specific to the Prophet.

The Hanafi Rule on Shara'i' Man Qablana
Usul debate on the authority of pre-Islamic sharia

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the binding nature of pre-Islamic laws and apply the criteria of 'narrated without disapproval' to determine binding force.

Core Idea
Previous prophets' laws bind Muslims only when endorsed without criticism by Quran or Sunnah.

This lesson examines a fundamental usul al-fiqh question: Are the religious laws given to prophets before Muhammad (peace be upon him) binding on the Muslim ummah? The Hanafi school presents a nuanced middle ground, distinguishing between independent authority and incorporation into Islamic sharia. Understanding this rule helps resolve apparent tensions between continuity and particularity of divine legislation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions
Read the first part to extract the three scholarly views: (1) all previous laws binding unless abrogated, (2) none binding unless proven, (3) binding as our own sharia.
The third opinion is the basis of the Hanafi position.
2
Analyse the Hanafi response
Note how the author declares the correct view: 'والصحيح عندنا...' focusing on the condition 'narrated without disapproval'.
This condition is key: it is not merely any narration, but absence of criticism.
3
Examine the proofs for each view
For the first view: Quran 6:90, 2:285, 5:48. For the second: 5:48 with takhsis. For the third (Hanafi): Quran 35:32 and the hadith about Umar and the Torah.
Pay attention to how each school uses the same verses differently.
4
Understand the Hanafi harmonisation
The Hanafi position does not reject previous laws entirely nor accept them all. It selects those that the Prophet (peace be upon him) or the Quran narrates without disapproval.
This avoids both extremes and respects the finality of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب شرائع من قبلنا
قال بعض العلماء: تلزمنا شرائعُ مَن قَبلنا حتى يقوم الدليل على النسخ، بمنزلة شرائعنا.
وقال بعضهم : لا تلزمنا حتى يقوم الدليل.
وقال بعضهم : تلزمنا على أنه شريعتنا.
والصحيح عندنا : أن ما قص الله تعالى منها علينا من غير إنكار، أو قصه رسولُ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من غير إنكار : فإنه يلزمنا، على أنه شريعة رسولنا عليه الصلاة والسلام.
احتج الأولون بقوله تبارك وتعالى: (أُوْلَيْكَ الَّذِينَ هَدَى اللَّهُ فَبِهُدَاهُمُ اقْتَدِهْ) (الأنعام / 90)، والهدى : اسم يقع على الإيمان والشرائع.
ولأنه ثبت حقيته دينا الله تبارك وتعالى ودين الله تعالى حَسَنٌ مرضي عنده، قال الله تبارك وتعالى: لَا نُفَرِّقُ بَيْنَ أَحَدٍ مِّن رُّسُلِهِ} (البقرة / 285)، وقال : {مُصَدِّقًا لِمَا بَيْنَ يَدَيْهِ مِنَ الْكِتَبِ وَمُهَيْمِنًا عَلَيْهِ} (المائدة / 48). فصار الأصل : هو الموافقة.
واحتج أهل المقالة الثانية : بقول الله تبارك وتعالى: { لِكُلِّ جَعَلْنَا مِنكُمْ شِرْعَةً وَمِنْهَاجًا} (المائدة / 48)؛ لأن الأصل في الشرائع الماضية: الخصوص، ألا يُرى أنها كانت تحتمل الخصوص في المكان في رسولين بُعِثا في زمان واحد في مكانين، إلا أن يكون أحدهما تبعاً للآخر، كما قال في قصة إبراهيم عليه السلام : {فَآمَنَ لَهُ لُوطٌ} (العنكبوت / 26). وكما كان هارون لموسى عليهما السلام، فكذلك في الزمان أيضاً، فصار الاختصاص في شرائعهم أصلاً، إلا بدليل.
واحتج أهل المقالة الثالثة بأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان أصلاً في الشرائع، وكانت شريعته عامةً لكافة الناس، وكان وارثاً لما مضى من محاسن الشريعة ومكارم الأخلاق، قال الله تبارك وتعالى: { ثُمَّ أَوْرَثْنَا الْكِتَبَ الَّذِينَ اصْطَفَيْنَا مِنْ عِبَادِنَا} (فاطر/32). ورأى رسولُ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في يد عمر رضي الله عنه صحيفة، فقال: ما هي؟ فقال: التوراة فقال: أمتهوكون أنتم كما تهوكت اليهود والنصارى، والله لو كان أخي موسى حياً لَمَا وَسِعَه إلا اتباعي )
Chapter on the laws of those before us. Some scholars said: The laws of those before us are binding upon us until evidence of abrogation is established, like our own laws. Some said: They are not binding upon us until evidence is established. Some said: They are binding upon us as our own law. The correct view with us is: That which Allah the Exalted narrated from them without disapproval, or which the Messenger of Allah narrated without disapproval, is binding upon us as the law of our Messenger, peace and blessings be upon him. The first group argued with His saying, Blessed and Exalted: (Those are the ones whom Allah has guided, so follow their guidance) (Al-An'am 6:90). And 'guidance' is a term that applies to faith and laws. And because it is established as the true religion of Allah, Blessed and Exalted, and the religion of Allah is good and pleasing to Him. Allah the Exalted said: (We make no distinction between any of His messengers) (Al-Baqarah 2:285), and said: (Confirming what came before it of the Scripture and a guardian over it) (Al-Ma'idah 5:48). So the principle is agreement. The proponents of the second opinion argued with the saying of Allah the Exalted: (For each of you We have appointed a law and a way) (Al-Ma'idah 5:48). Because the principle in the past laws is particularization. Do you not see that they could bear particularization in place concerning two messengers sent in one time in two places, unless one of them is a follower of the other, as He said in the story of Abraham, peace be upon him: (And Lot believed in him) (Al-Ankabut 29:26); and as Aaron was to Moses, peace be upon them. So likewise in time, particularization became the principle in their laws, unless there is evidence. The proponents of the third opinion argued that the Prophet, peace be upon him, was the origin in laws, and his law was general for all people, and he was the inheritor of the noble traits and good ethics of the past. Allah the Exalted said: (Then We gave the Scripture as inheritance to those whom We selected from among Our servants) (Fatir 35:32). And the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, saw in the hand of Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, a leaf and said: 'What is this?' He said: The Torah. He said: 'Are you confounded as the Jews and Christians were confounded? By Allah, if my brother Moses were alive, he would have no choice but to follow me.'
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage is a usul al-fiqh discussion on whether the religious laws of prophets before Muhammad (peace be upon him) are binding on the Muslim ummah. The author presents three scholarly opinions. The first opinion holds that all previous sharia laws are binding on Muslims unless there is clear evidence of abrogation (naskh). Their proof includes the verse commanding to follow the guidance of the previous prophets, and the principle that Allah's religion is one and good. The second opinion argues that previous laws are not binding at all unless there is explicit evidence from the Quran or Sunnah that they apply to us. They rely on the verse 'For each of you We have appointed a law and a way', indicating that each community has its own distinct sharia; therefore specificity is assumed. The third opinion states that previous laws are binding, but as our own sharia (shari'atuna), not as a separate law. The author then declares the correct Hanafi position: that what Allah or the Prophet narrates from previous prophets without criticism is binding, but as part of the sharia of our Prophet (peace be upon him). This is based on the verse about inheriting the Scripture and the hadith where the Prophet rebuked Umar for having the Torah and declared that even Prophet Moses would follow him. Thus, the Hanafi view is that selected elements of previous laws are incorporated into Islamic sharia, not followed independently.

Key Terms
شرائع من قبلنا (shara'i' man qablana): The religious laws revealed to prophets before Muhammad (peace be upon him).
الهدى (al-huda): Guidance, encompasses both faith and legal rulings.
على أنه شريعتنا ('ala annahu shari'atuna): Binding as our own law, not as a separate law from another prophet.
النسخ (al-naskh): Abrogation: the replacement of a legal ruling by a later revelation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, which of the following is true about the laws of previous prophets?
A) They are all binding until abrogated.
B) They are never binding on Muslims.
C) They are binding only if narrated by Allah or the Prophet without disapproval, as part of our Prophet's law.
D) They are binding as independent laws of previous prophets.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school teaches that previous prophets' laws are binding on Muslims only when the Quran or Sunnah narrates them without disapproval, and even then they are binding as part of the sharia of Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him), not as independent laws.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Refuting Qiyas on the Prophet's Special Rulings (Khawass al-Nabi)
Dispute over applying qiyas to Prophet's special rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that khawass al-Nabi cannot be invalidated by qiyas and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the madhhab dispute map.

Core Idea
Special rulings (khawass) granted by nass to the Prophet are immune to qiyas.

This lesson examines the Hanafi argument that specific rulings exclusive to the Prophet (peace be upon him) cannot be extended or annulled by analogical reasoning (qiyas). The author provides textual proofs and responds to Imam al-Shafi'i's differing view. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to locate where qiyas is valid and where it is blocked by the authority of nass.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested principle
What exactly is being disputed? The core issue is whether special rulings (khawass) of the Prophet can be annulled or extended by qiyas.
The passage states: 'فلم يستقم إبطال الخصوص بالتعليل' (invalidating the specific by reasoning is not sound).
2
Locate the Hanafi dalil
Find the textual proofs cited: the hadith of salam (fixed measure, weight, term) and the ruling on ijarah (leasing) established by nass.
These are presented as khawass that cannot be analogized.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to Shafi'i
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the Prophet's marriage by hibah is a khass, but can be subject to qiyas. The Hanafi response is that the specificity lies in the prohibition for others after him, not in the wording; the honor (karamah) protects it from qiyas.
Key: 'الاختصاص في سلامتها له بغير عوض، وفي اختصاصه بأن لا تحل لأحد بعده'
4
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
What practical difference does this dispute make? Hanafis do not apply qiyas to derive rulings from the Prophet's marriages; Shafi'is may do so.
E.g., Hanafis require dower in all marriages; Shafi'is might allow exceptions based on the hibah precedent.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Why is the Hanafi position relied upon? Because nass overrides qiyas, and the khawass are established by definitive textual evidence.
The principle: 'ما ثبت بنص لا يبطل بالقياس'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلم يصح إبطاله بالتعليل. تعليله. وحل للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم تسع نسوة؛ إكراماً له، فلم يصح وكذلك ثبت بالنص أن البيع يقتضي محلاً مملوكاً مقدوراً. وجوز السلم في الدين بالنص، وهو قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «من أسلم منكم فليسلم في كيل معلوم، ووزن معلوم إلى أجل معلوم». وما ثبت بهذا النص إلا مؤجلاً، فلم يستقم إبطال الخصوص بالتعليل. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله: لما صح نكاح النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام بلفظة الهبة على سبيل الخصوص بقوله تعالى: {خالصة لك}. بطل التعليل. وقلنا: بل الاختصاص في سلامتها له بغير عوض، وفي اختصاصه بأن لا تحل لأحد بعده، قال الله تعالى: {وأزواجه أمهاتهم} الأحزاب/6. وقال: {قد علمنا ما فرضنا عليهم في أزواجهم} الأحزاب/50، وهذا مما يعقل كرامةً، فأما الاختصاص باللفظ: فلا. وقد أبطلنا التعليل من حيث ثبت كرامة. وكذلك ثبت للمنافع حكم التقوم والمالية في باب عقود الإجارة بالنص، مخالفاً للقياس المعقول؛ لأن التقوم والتمول يعتمد الوجود ليصلح للإحراز، والتقوم عبارة عن اعتدال المعاني، وبين العين والمنافع تفاوت في نفس الوجود، فلا يصح إبطال الخصوص بالتعليل. ومثال الثاني من الشروط: أن أكل الناسي معدول به عن القياس، وهو فوات القربة بما يضاد ركنها هو القياس المحض، وثبت حكم
So it is not correct to invalidate it by reasoning. Its reasoning: The Prophet was allowed nine wives as an honor for him, so it is not correct. Likewise, it is established by the text that sale requires an owned and possessed object. Salam (advance payment) was permitted in debts by the text, which is his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever among you gives salam, let him give salam in a known measure, known weight, and a known term.' And nothing is established by this text except deferred, so invalidating the specific ruling by reasoning was not sound. And al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: When the marriage of the Prophet, peace be upon him, was valid with the word of hibah as a specific ruling by His saying, Most High: {Exclusively for you}. The reasoning is invalid. And we said: Rather, the specificity is in her being permissible for him without compensation, and in his specificity that she is not permissible for anyone after him. Allah Most High said: {And his wives are their mothers} al-Ahzab/6. And He said: {We know what We prescribed for them regarding their wives} al-Ahzab/50. And this is understood as an honor. As for specificity in wording, no. And we have invalidated the reasoning because it is established as an honor. Likewise, it is established for benefits the ruling of value and monetary worth in the chapter of ijarah contracts by the text, contrary to acceptable analogy; because valuation and monetization depend on existence to be suitable for possession, and valuation is an expression of balance of meanings, and between the substance and benefits there is a difference in existence itself, so it is not correct to invalidate the specific ruling by reasoning. And an example of the second type of conditions: that the eating of one who forgets is turned away from analogy, and the loss of worship by what contradicts its pillar is pure analogy, and the ruling is established.
Source pages p. 573

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul al-fiqh commentary refuting the use of qiyas (analogical reasoning) to extend or invalidate specific rulings (khawass) granted to the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). The author begins with the principle that special privileges—like the permission for nine wives—cannot be overturned by reasoning. He then provides two textual proofs: first, the permissibility of salam (advance payment) is established by a direct hadith that specifies measure, weight, and term, making it a khass ruling. Second, the permissibility of ijarah (leasing contracts) is also established by nass, despite being contrary to qiyas, because benefits (manafi) are not physically present at the time of contract yet are given value. The author then critiques Imam al-Shafi'i's argument that the Prophet's marriage by hibah (gift) is a khass but can be subject to qiyas. The Hanafi response clarifies that the specificity lies in the prohibition for others after him, not in the wording of the contract. Finally, the author gives an example from conditions of prayer: the eating of a fasting person who forgets is an exception from qiyas, as the general rule would nullify the fast. This shows that rulings from nass override qiyas.

Key Terms
الخصوص (al-khussus): A specific ruling granted exclusively to the Prophet, not applicable to the ummah.
التعليل (al-ta'lil): Reasoning by analogy (qiyas) to extend or annul a ruling.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to derive rulings for new cases from an original case with a shared cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in the passage, why can the Prophet's special privilege of having nine wives not be invalidated by qiyas?
A) Because the wives were all widows, so the ruling is specific to that context.
B) Because the permission is established by nass as an honor (karamah) and cannot be analogized.
C) Because the number nine is too high for the ummah.
D) Because Abu Hanifa opposed polygamy.

Key Takeaway
Khawass al-Nabi established by explicit nass are immune to qiyas; only a stronger nass can override them.

The Riba Example: Text-Based Takhsis in the Hanafi Usul
Shafi'i objection and Hanafi response on ta'lil vs nass

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection that ta'lil overrides nass, and apply the concept of istithna to identify when particularization is based on wording rather than independent reasoning.

Core Idea
Hanafi particularization of the riba text is based on the wording's implicit exception, not arbitrary reasoning.

Imam al-Shafi'i argued that the Hanafi school uses legal reasoning (ta'lil) to change explicit textual rulings, citing several examples. The Hanafi response, as presented by al-Bazdawi, clarifies that the particularization (takhsis) in the riba example is not from independent reasoning but from the wording (sighah) of the text itself. This lesson dissects this methodological dispute and demonstrates how the distinction between text-based and reason-based rulings is central to Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Read the passage and list the four examples where Imam al-Shafi'i claims the Hanafis used ta'lil to override explicit textual rulings (nass).
Focus on the riba example as the one the Hanafi response addresses.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response to the riba example
The Hanafi response states that the particularization is a 'wahm' (misunderstanding). The key claim is that the particularization is not from ta'lil but from the wording (sighah) of the text itself. Identify the specific linguistic structure mentioned: istithna (exception).
The response uses an example from al-Jami' to illustrate how the excepted-from (mustathna minhu) is understood from the excepted (mustathna).
3
Understand the istithna mechanism
In the riba hadith 'Do not sell food for food', the general prohibition is accompanied by an implicit exception for trivial amounts. Just as in the example 'If there is no one in the house except Zaid, my slave is free' the excepted-from is understood as 'sons of Adam,' here the prohibition implicitly excepts negligible quantities. The wording (negation + except) creates a bounded generality.
This mechanism shows that the particularization is not a free-standing reasoning but a determination of the text's scope.
4
Apply the principle to a new case
Given a similar textual structure (e.g., 'La tu'ajjiru al-masakin illa bi ajrin ma'qul'), determine the mustathna minhu and derive the ruling. This practices recognizing text-based takhsis.
Use the same logic: the exception defines the boundary of the prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : أنتم غيّرتُم حكم النص بالتعليل في مسائل:
منها: أن نص الربا يَعُمُّ القليل والكثير، وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «لا تبيعوا الطعام بالطعام» (١) ، فخَصَّصتم منها القليل بالتعليل. والنص أوجب الشاة(۲) فى الزكاة بصورتها ومعناها، فأبطلتم الحق عن صورتها بالتعليل، والحقُّ المستحق مراعى بصورته ومعناه، كما في حقوق العياد.
وأوجب النص الزكاة للأصناف الثمانية المسميين بقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّمَا الصَّدَقَتُ لِلْفُقَرَاءِ ... الآية، التوبة / ٦٠ ، وقد أبطلتموه بجواز الصرف إلى صنف واحد بطريق التعليل. وأوجب الشرعُ التكبير لافتتاح الصلاة)، وعين الماء لغسل العين النَّجسة) ، وقد أبطلتم هذا الواجب بالتعليل.
والجواب : أن هذا وَهُم.
أما الأول فلأن الخصوص إنما ثبت بصيغة النص، وذلك لأن المستثنى منه إنما يثبت على وفق المستثنى فيما استثني من النفي. كما قال في «الجامع»: إن كان في الدار إلا زيد فعبدي حر: إن المستثنى منه بنو آدم.
And al-Shafi'i, may God have mercy on him, said: "You have changed the ruling of the explicit text by means of reasoning (ta'lil) in several issues: Among them: that the text on riba is general encompassing small and large amounts, and it is his saying, peace be upon him: 'Do not sell food for food' (1), so you specified from it the small amount by reasoning. And the text obligated the sheep (2) in zakat by its form and meaning, so you nullified the right regarding its form by reasoning, while the right due is observed by its form and meaning, as in the rights of servants. And the text obligated zakat for the eight named categories by His saying, exalted: 'Indeed the charities are for the poor...' [verse, At-Tawbah 60], and you have nullified it by permitting disbursement to a single category by way of reasoning. And the law obligated the takbir for opening the prayer) and designated water for washing the impure eye), and you have nullified this obligation by reasoning." And the response: That this is a misunderstanding. As for the first, the particularization is only established by the wording of the text, because the excepted-from is only established in accordance with the excepted in what is excluded from negation. As it is said in al-Jami': 'If there is no one in the house except Zaid, then my slave is free: the excepted-from is the sons of Adam.'
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage presents a classic usul dispute. Imam al-Shafi'i (founder of the Shafi'i school) objects to the Hanafi use of ta'lil (legal reasoning) to override explicit textual commands (nass). He provides four examples: (1) the general prohibition of selling food for food (riba hadith) which Hanafis particularized to allow small amounts; (2) the obligation of a sheep in zakat which Hanafis changed based on reasoning; (3) the eight categories of zakat recipients which Hanafis allowed disbursement to one; (4) the takbir for opening prayer and water for washing impurities which Hanafis altered. The Hanafi response states this is a misunderstanding (wahm). They address only the first example: the particularization (takhsis) is not from independent reasoning but from the wording (sighah) of the text itself. They explain using the concept of istithna' (exception): in the hadith 'Do not sell food for food', the structure implies an exception for small amounts, similar to the legal example from al-Jami' where the excepted-from (mustathna minhu) is determined by the excepted (mustathna). Thus, the Hanafi ruling on riba is based on textual analysis, not arbitrary reasoning.

Key Terms
التعليل (al-ta'lil): Legal reasoning based on causation (illa) to extend or restrict rulings
تخصيص (takhsis): Particularization: restricting a general ('amm) ruling to some of its individuals
نص (nass): An explicit text (Quran or hadith) that is clear and decisive in meaning
صيغة (sighah): The wording or grammatical form of a text
مستثنى منه (mustathna minhu): The excepted-from: the general class from which an exception is made

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response, how is the particularization of the riba hadith established?
A) By independent rational reasoning (ta'lil) without textual basis.
B) By the wording (sighah) of the text through the structure of exception (istithna).
C) By the consensus (ijma) of the companions.
D) By following a weaker hadith.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi particularization of the riba hadith is text-based, grounded in the wording's implicit exception structure, not independent reasoning.

How Exception Determines the Scope of General Terms; Zakah as Pure Worship
Explaining the rule of exception and refuting overuse of rationalization

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for determining the mustathna minhu based on the category of the mustathna, and distinguish between rulings subject to ta'lil and pure acts of worship such as zakah.

Core Idea
The mustathna minhu is determined by the type of the excepted item, not by a universal set.

This passage from Al-Bazdawi's Usul tackles a subtle objection regarding the role of rationalization in determining legal obligations. It uses the linguistic mechanism of exception (istithna) to show how the scope of a general term is shaped by the specific item being excepted. The passage then contrasts this with the case of zakah, which is a pure act of worship ('ibada mahda) beyond rationalization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle of istithna
Recognize that the mustathna minhu (the term from which exception is made) corresponds to the category of the mustathna (the excepted item). This is a linguistic rule derived from the wording, not from independent reasoning.
E.g., 'no one except Zaid' → mustathna minhu = humans; 'except a donkey' → mustathna minhu = animals.
2
Apply the principle to examples
Work through the examples given in the text: 'except Zaid' → humans; 'except a donkey' → animals; 'except furniture' → everything. Note how the category of the mustathna determines the scope.
The example 'except furniture' shows that when the excepted item is a broad category, the mustathna minhu becomes everything, i.e., the universal set.
3
Analyze the hadith 'except equal for equal'
The exception in the hadith is a state (equality) from physical objects. Excepting a state from entities is invalid in reality, so the generality of the first part must be established regarding states. The exception then exempts only the state of equality, leaving other states (excess, arbitrariness) under the general prohibition.
This shows that the change in ruling is due to the text itself (the exception), not due to rationalization.
4
Understand why zakah is not subject to ta'lil
Zakah is a pure act of worship (ibada mahda), a right of Allah, not a right of the poor. Allah promised provision for the poor, imposed a specific wealth on the rich, and commanded fulfillment from that wealth. Changes in fulfillment (e.g., substitution) are by textual permission, not rationalization.
The analogy with the sultan's deputies illustrates that the obligation is fixed by divine command, not by the changing needs of the poor.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وها هنا استثنى الحال بقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «إلا سواء بسواء»(۱)، واستثناء الحال من الأعيان باطل في الحقيقة، فوجب أن يثبت عموم صدره في الأحوال بهذه الدلالة، وهو حال التساوي والتفاضل والمجازفة، ثم استثنى منها حال التساوي، ولن يثبت اختلاف الأحوال إلا في الكثير ، فصار التغيير بالنص مصاحباً بالتعليل، لا به.

the impurity and you have invalidated this obligation by rationalization. The answer: This is a mistake. As for the first (point), because specification (khusus) is only established by the wording of the text, and that is because the term from which exception is made (mustathna minhu) is only established in accordance with the excepted item (mustathna) in what is excepted from negation. As it is said in al-Jami': 'If there is no one in the house except Zaid, then my slave is free': the mustathna minhu is human beings. (Hadith quotes about purification and prayer) (Footnote: Statement: 'And water for washing the impure part' from Asma bint Abi Bakr...) And if he said: 'except a donkey', the mustathna minhu would be animals because the excepted item is an animal. And if he said: 'except furniture', the mustathna minhu would be everything. And here, the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم excepted a state by his saying: 'except equal for equal', and excepting a state from physical objects is invalid in reality, so it was necessary that the generality of the beginning of the statement be established regarding states by this indication, which are the states of equality, excess, and arbitrariness. Then he excepted from them the state of equality, and differences in states are only established in quantity, so the change by text accompanies rationalization, not by it. As for zakah, there is no obligatory right for the poor that changes by rationalization, because zakah is a pure act of worship, so it is not obligatory for the creatures in any way. Rather, the obligation is the right of Allah Almighty, and His right is only dropped in form by His permission through the text, not by rationalization, because He promised provision for the poor. Then He imposed a specified wealth on the rich for Himself, then commanded us to fulfill the promises from that specified wealth, and that cannot bear differences in promises except by substitution, like the sultan who authorizes his friends with promises written in their names, then commands some of his deputies to fulfill them from a specific wealth, that was permission for substitution. Thus, change is accompanied by rationalization through the text, not by rationalization itself. Rather, rationalization applies to a legal ruling, which is that the sheep is suitable for delivery to the poor, and this is a legal ruling, and its explanation: that the sheep becomes with Allah Almighty by the beginning of the poor's possession a purification (qurba mutahhira), so it becomes from impurities, like used water.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of an objection and its refutation regarding the role of exception (istithna) in determining general terms (umum) and the application of rationalization (ta'lil) to legal obligations, specifically in the context of zakah. The author begins by addressing an opponent who claimed that a certain obligation (likely concerning impurity) was invalidated by rationalization. The author counters that this is a mistake. The first point is that specification (khusus) is derived from the wording of the text, not from rationalization. The principle is that the term from which exception is made (mustathna minhu) is determined by the nature of the excepted item (mustathna). For example, if one says 'There is no one in the house except Zaid,' the mustathna minhu is 'human beings' because Zaid is a human. Similarly, if one says 'except a donkey,' the mustathna minhu is 'animals'; if 'except furniture,' it is 'everything.' The text then applies this to a hadith: 'except equal for equal' (a hadith regarding riba). Here, the exception is of a state (equality) from physical objects, which is invalid in reality. Therefore, the generality of the first part of the hadith (which implies all exchanges must be equal?) is established regarding states: equality, excess, and arbitrariness. Then the exception of equality shows that the ruling changes due to the text, not due to rationalization. The author then turns to zakah, arguing that it is a pure act of worship ('ibada mahda). Unlike other obligations that may be subject to rationalization (like the impurity ruling), zakah is not a right of the poor that can change based on rationale. Instead, it is a right of Allah. Allah promised provision for the poor, imposed a specific wealth on the rich, and commanded us to fulfill those promises from that wealth. This can only accommodate different promises through substitution (istibdal), illustrated by the analogy of a sultan authorizing his deputies to fulfill promises from a specific fund. Thus, the change (e.g., in the quantity of zakah) is by text, not by independent rationalization. The rationalization mentioned (e.g., the sheep being suitable for delivery) is for a legal ruling (hukm shar'i) that the sheep becomes a purifying act of worship upon delivery, similar to used water becoming impure.

Key Terms
المستثنى منه (al-mustathna minhu): The general term from which exception is made; its scope is determined by the nature of the excepted item.
التعليل (al-ta'lil): Rationalization of a legal ruling based on an underlying cause or wisdom.
عبادة محضة (ibada mahda): A pure act of worship with no worldly cause or right of created beings; its rulings are based solely on divine command.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Animals (al-hayawan)
B) Camels (al-ibil)
C) Everything (kull shay')
D) Livestock (al-mawashi)

Key Takeaway
The mustathna minhu is determined by the category of the mustathna, and pure acts of worship like zakah are not subject to rationalization.

Excepting a State from Physical Objects in Hanafi Usul
Distinguishing the exception of state in hadith from zakat's fixed obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that excepting a state (ḥāl) from physical objects (aʿyān) does not restrict the general term, and derive the ruling that all states of sale except equality remain covered by the hadith.

Core Idea
Excepting a state from physical objects is invalid; the general term continues to apply to all states except the explicitly excepted one.

In this passage, the author explains a key usul principle about the exception (istithna) of a state or condition. When the Prophet (ṣ) said 'except equal for equal', he excepted a state from physical objects, which is logically invalid. Consequently, the general term remains operative over all other states. This lesson will examine how this rule impacts the interpretation of the hadith on sales and why it cannot be applied to zakat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the istithna in the hadith
The Prophet (ṣ) excepted the state of equality from the general permissibility of selling dates for dates.
2
Recognize the principle
Excepting a state from physical objects (aʿyān) is invalid in reality (bāṭil fī al-ḥaqīqa).
This is a Hanafi specific rule.
3
Apply the rule
The general term (selling at any value) must be applied to all states except the excepted one (equality). Thus, disparity and lump-sum remain valid.
4
Note the text-bound nature
The change (tabyīn) is by the text, not by rational cause (taʿlīl). The exception is purely textual.
5
Contrast with zakat (preview)
This textual exception does not apply to zakat because zakat is a pure act of worship with no changeable right for the poor.
Zakat will be detailed in the next lesson.

ولو قال : إلا حمار كان المستثنى منه الحيوان، لأن المستثنى حيوان ولو قال : إلا متاع كان المستثنى منه كل شيء. وها هنا استثنى الحال بقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «إلا سواء بسواء»، واستثناء الحال من الأعيان باطل في الحقيقة، فوجب أن يثبت عموم صدره في الأحوال بهذه الدلالة، وهو حال التساوي والتفاضل والمجازفة، ثم استثنى منها حال التساوي، ولن يثبت اختلاف الأحوال إلا في الكثير ، فصار التغيير بالنص مصاحباً بالتعليل، لا به. وأما الزكاة فليس فيها حق واجب للفقير يتغير بالتعليل؛ لأن الزكاة عبادة محضة فلا تجب للعباد ،بوجه، وإنما الواجب حق الله تعالى، وإنما سقط حقه في الصورة بإذنه بالنص، لا بالتعليل، لأنه تعالى وَعَدَ إرزاق الفقراء. ثم أوجب مالاً مسمى على الأغنياء لنفسه، ثم أمرنا بإنجاز المواعيد من ذلك المسمى، وذلك لا يحتمله مع اختلاف المواعيد إلا بالاستبدال كالسلطان يُجيز لأوليائه بمواعيد كتبها بأسمائهم، ثم أَمَرَ بعض وكلائه بأن يُنجزها من مال بعينه كان إذناً بالاستبدال، فصار التغيير مجامعاً للتعليل بالنص، لا بالتعليل. وإنما التعليل لحكم شرعي، وهو كون الشاة صالحة للتسليم إلى الفقير، وهذا حكم شرعي وبيانُه : أن الشاة تقع الله تعالى بابتداء قبض الفقير قُربةً مطهّرةً، فتصير من الأوساخ ، كالماء المستعمل. قال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام : يا بني هاشم ! إن الله تعالى كَرِه لكم أوساخ الناس، وعوضكم منها بخُمُس الخُمس. وقد كانت النار تنزل من السماء في الأمم الماضية فتحرق المتقبَّلَ الصدقات، وأُحلّت لهذه الأمة بعد أن ثبت خُبْتُها بشرط الحاجة من والضرورة، كما تحلّ الميتة بالضرورة ، وحُرّمت على الغني، فصار صلاح الصرف إلى الفقير بعد الوقوع الله تعالى بابتداء اليد؛ ليصير مصروفاً إلى الفقير بدوام يده حكماً شرعياً الشاة، فعللناه بالتقويم، وعديناه إلى في سائر الأموال على موافقة سائر العلل. ولما ثبت أن الواجب خالص حق الله تعالى : كان اللام في قوله تعالى: لِلْفُقَرَاء ( التوبة / ٦٠: لامَ العاقبة ، أي يصير لهم لعاقبته، أو لأنه أوجب لهم بعد ما صار صدقةً، وذلك بعد الأداء إلى الله تعالى، فصاروا على هذا التحقيق مصارف باعتبار الحاجة. وهذه الأسماء أسباب الحاجة، وهم بجملتهم للزكاة: مثل الكعبة للصلاة، وكل صنف منهم مثل جزء من الكعبة واستقبال جزء من الكعبة جائز، كاستقبال كلها، فكذلك ها هنا.
And if he said: except a donkey, then the excluded-from is the animal, because the excepted is an animal. And if he said: except goods, then the excluded-from is everything. And here he excepted the state by his statement, peace be upon him: 'except equal for equal', and excepting the state from physical objects is invalid in reality, so it is necessary that the generality of its first part is established in the states by this indication, that is the state of equality, disparity, and lump-sum bargaining, then he excepted from them the state of equality, and the difference of states is not established except in the many, so the change by the text accompanies the rational cause, not by it. As for zakat, there is no obligatory right for the poor that changes by rational cause, because zakat is a pure act of worship, so it is not obligatory for the servants in any way, and the obligatory is only the right of Allah the Exalted, and His right is dropped in form by His permission through the text, not by rational cause, because He the Exalted promised the sustenance of the poor. Then He imposed a specified wealth on the rich for Himself, then He commanded us to fulfill the promises from that specified wealth, and that cannot bear the difference of promises except by substitution, like the Sultan authorizes his favorites with promises written in their names, then he ordered some of his deputies to fulfill them from a specific wealth, that was permission for substitution, so the change accompanies the rational cause by the text, not by the rational cause. And the rational cause is for a legal ruling, which is the sheep being suitable for delivery to the poor, and this is a legal ruling and its explanation: that the sheep falls to Allah the Exalted by the initial taking of the poor as a purifying act of worship, so it becomes from the filth, like used water. The Prophet, peace be upon him, said: O Banu Hashim! Indeed Allah the Exalted dislikes for you the filth of people, and compensated you for it with the fifth of the fifth. And the fire used to descend from the sky in past nations and burn the accepted charities, and it was permitted for this nation after its filth was established under the condition of need and necessity, just as carrion is permitted by necessity, and it was forbidden for the rich, so the validity of disbursement to the poor after occurrence by Allah the Exalted by the initial hand, so that it becomes disbursed to the poor by the permanence of his hand as a legal ruling of the sheep, so we gave rational cause for it by valuation, and we extended it to the rest of the wealth in accordance with the rest of the causes. And when it was established that the obligatory is purely the right of Allah the Exalted, the lam in His statement: 'for the poor' (At-Tawba 60) is the lam of consequence, meaning it becomes for them in its consequence, or because He made it obligatory for them after it became charity, and that after payment to Allah the Exalted, so they on this verification become recipients due to need. And these names are causes of need, and they in their entirety are for zakat like the Kaaba for prayer, and each category of them is like a part of the Kaaba, and facing a part of the Kaaba is permissible, like facing all of it, so likewise here.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage is a comparative usul analysis between two contexts: the exception (istithna) in the hadith 'except equal for equal' regarding sales, and the ruling of zakat. The author begins by explaining the principle of istithna: when someone says 'I sell you this animal except a donkey', the excepted item (donkey) determines the category (animal). But here, the Prophet (SAW) excepted a state (hal) from physical objects, which is logically invalid, so the general term (all states of equality, disparity, and lump-sum) remains except the explicitly excepted state (equality). Thus, change in ruling is by the text itself, not by rational cause. For zakat, the argument is different: there is no established right for the poor that varies by rational cause, because zakat is a pure act of worship owed solely to Allah. Allah promised sustenance to the poor and then imposed a specific wealth on the rich for Himself. Fulfilling that promise from that wealth requires substitution (istibdal), not rational adjustment. The rational cause (ta'lil) is only for the legal ruling that the sheep becomes suitable for delivery to the poor as a purifying act, making it like 'filth' (used water). The hadith about Banu Hashim further illustrates the concept of filth. The author concludes that the lam in 'lil-fuqara' is the lam of consequence, meaning the recipients are determined by need, similar to facing parts of the Kaaba. This is a classic Hanafi usul argument emphasizing the text-bound nature of zakat obligations.

Key Terms
استثناء الحال (istithnā' al-ḥāl): Exception of a state or condition from a general term that refers to physical objects.
التعليل (al-taʿlīl): Rational cause (ʿilla) used to extend or modify a ruling based on a common attribute.
لام العاقبة (lām al-ʿāqibah): The lām that indicates the consequence or ultimate outcome, not immediate ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that excepting a state from physical objects is 'invalid in reality'?
A) Because the exception is not explicitly mentioned in the Quran.
B) Because physical objects and states are ontologically distinct, so the exception cannot logically apply.
C) Because the hadith is weak.
D) Because the general term is not applicable to states.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, excepting a state from physical objects does not restrict the general; the exception only removes that specific state, leaving the general covering all others.

Zakat: A Pure Act of Worship Not Subject to Rational Cause
Distinguishing the exception of state in hadith from zakat's fixed obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the obligation of zakat cannot be modified by rational analogy (taʿlīl) and identify the meaning of lām al-ʿāqibah in verse 9:60, applying it to understand the poor's right in zakat.

Core Idea
Zakat is a pure worship owed to Allah; the poor have no pre-existing right that can be adjusted by rational analogy.

This lesson examines the second part of the passage, where the author argues that zakat is fundamentally different from sales. He states that zakat is a pure act of worship (ʿibāda maḥḍa) owed solely to Allah. The right of the poor is determined by divine command, not by rational cause (taʿlīl). He then explains the verse 'lil-fuqarā' using the concept of lām al-ʿāqibah, indicating that the poor become recipients only after the payment is made to Allah. This lesson will clarify the text-bound nature of zakat and its implications for rulings.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the claim
In zakat, there is no obligatory right for the poor that changes by reason (taʿlīl). Zakat is a pure act of worship, so it is not owed to people in any way. The obligation is only Allah's right.
2
Understand the mechanism
Allah promised sustenance to the poor and imposed a specified wealth on the rich for Himself. Fulfilling that promise from that wealth requires substitution (istibdāl), not rational adjustment.
The sultan analogy illustrates this.
3
Distinguish the role of taʿlīl
Taʿlīl applies only to the legal ruling that the sheep becomes suitable for delivery as a purifyer, not to the core obligation.
The rational cause is for a subsidiary ruling, not the obligation itself.
4
Analyze the verse 'lil-fuqarā'
The lām is lām al-ʿāqibah (consequence), meaning the poor ultimately receive it, not that it is owed to them. Their need makes them recipients.
Analogous to facing any part of the Kaaba.
5
Apply to the overall argument
The text-bound nature of zakat means its rulings cannot be analogized from sales or other muʿāmalāt.

Commentary

Thus, change in ruling is by the text itself, not by rational cause. Key Takeaway

The Principle of Daf' Rebutting Objections in Qiyas
Explaining daf' as a method to counter criticisms in analogical reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi method of daf' to rebut objections in qiyas by identifying whether the objection is a tard (consistent application) or a prevented case (mani'), and construct a proper rebuttal.

Core Idea
Daf' rebuts objections by showing either consistent application (tard) or a preventer (mani') — preserving the analogy.

When deriving rulings by analogy, a critic may object that the ruling does not hold in a parallel case. The Hanafi usul tradition teaches that such objections can be rebutted through daf' — a method that distinguishes between genuine refutations and irrelevant counterexamples. This lesson unpacks the two main forms of daf': tard (consistent application) and the identification of a mani' (preventer). Through concrete examples from the sources, you will learn to apply this tool in your own istinbat practice.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish tard from naqd
First, understand that daf' operates by showing that an objection is either tard (the analogy applies consistently, so the objection is not a real counterexample) or is blocked by a mani' (a preventer that explains why the ruling does not apply, even though the cause exists).
The text explicitly contrasts tard with naqd (refutation).
2
Analyze the examples for type of daf'
Work through each example in the passage: urine, ghasp, attacking camel, istihadah blood, and amin. For each, identify the original analogy, the objection, and whether the Hanafi response uses tard or a mani'.
Some examples use tard (camel case), others use mani' (istihadah).
3
Extract the underlying principle
From the examples, derive the general rule: daf' does not depart from the original analogy; it only clarifies that the objection fails because it overlooks a barrier or because the same reasoning holds consistently.
The text says 'لا يُفارق الأصل' (it does not depart from the origin).
4
Apply daf' to a new scenario
Test your understanding by taking a new qiyas scenario and constructing a daf' rebuttal using either tard or mani'.
Use the practice tasks for application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموضع ، فصار بمعنى البول ، وهذا غير خارج إذا لم يسل، حتى لم يتعلق به وجوب التطهير. وأما الدفع بالحكم فمثل قولنا في الغصب : إنه سبب لملك البدل، فكان سبباً لملك المُبدّل. ولا يلزم المدبَّرُ؛ لأنا جعلناه سبباً فيه أيضاً، لكنه امتنع حكمه لمانع، كالبيع يضاف إليه. ومثل قولنا في الجَمَل الصائل: إن المَصُّولَ عليه أتلفه لإحياء نفسه، والاستحلال لإحياء المهجة لا ينافي عصمة المتلف، كما إذا أتلفه دفعاً للمخمصة. ولا يلزم مالُ الباغي، وما يجري مجراه؛ لأن عصمته لم تبطل بهذا المعنى، فكان طرداً، لا نقضاً. 29 وكذلك متى قلنا في الدم إنه نجس خارج، فكان حدثاً : لم يلزم دم الاستحاضة؛ لأنه حدث أيضاً؛ لكن عمله امتنع لمانع. وأما الرابع فمثل قولنا نجس خارج. ولا يلزم دم المستحاضة، ودمُ صاحب الجرح السائل الدائم؛ لأن غرضنا التسوية بين هذا وبين الخارج من المخرج المعتاد، وذلك حَدَثُ، فإذا لزم صار عفواً ؛ لقيام وقت الصلاة، فكذلك هذا. وكذلك قولنا في التأمين: إنه ذكر، فكان سبيله الإخفاء. - ولا يلزم عليه الأذان، وتكبيرات الإمام ؛ لأن غرضنا أن أصل الذكر و الإخفاء. وكذلك أصل الأذان ،والتكبيرات إلا أن في تلك الأذكار معنىً زائداً، وهو أنها إعلام، فلذلك أوجب فيها حكماً عارضاً، ألا يُرى أن المنفرد والمقتدي لا يجهر بالتكبير ، ومَن صلى وحده أذَن لنفسه. وهذا معنى قول مشايخنا رحمهم الله في الدفع : إنه لا يُفارق الأصل، لكن ما قلنا : أبين في وجوه الدفع، والله أعلم. وإذا قامت المعارضة : كان السبيل فيه الترجيح. وهذا باب الترجيح
The place, so it became equivalent to urine, and this is not excluded if it does not flow, so that the obligation of purification is not attached to it. As for rebuttal by ruling, it is like our statement regarding usurpation: that it is a cause for ownership of the substitute, so it became a cause for ownership of the original. And the thing managed (by the master's death) is not entailed; because we made it a cause in it also, but its ruling was prevented by a barrier, like sale is added to it. And like our statement regarding the attacking camel: that the one attacked destroyed it to save his life, and taking it as permissible to save one's life does not negate the inviolability of the one destroyed, as when one destroys it to ward off starvation. And the property of the rebel is not entailed, and what runs in its course; because his inviolability is not nullified by this meaning, so it is consistent application (tard), not refutation (naqd). 29 Likewise, whenever we say about blood that it is impure fluid, so it is an event (hadath): the blood of istihadah is not entailed; because it is also an event; but its effect is prevented by a barrier. As for the fourth, it is like our statement: impure fluid. And the blood of the woman with istihadah and the blood of the person with a continuously flowing wound are not entailed; because our purpose is equalization between this and what exits from the usual exit, and that is an event; so if it is entailed, it becomes excused due to the time of prayer, so this is similar. And likewise our statement regarding the 'amin': that it is a dhikr, so its way is concealment. - And the adhan and the takbirs of the imam are not entailed upon it; because our purpose is that the essence of dhikr is concealment. And likewise the essence of adhan and takbirs, except that in those remembrances there is an additional meaning, which is that they are announcements, therefore a temporary ruling is necessitated in them. Do you not see that the one praying alone and the one following do not pronounce the takbir aloud, and whoever prays alone calls the adhan for himself. And this is the meaning of our predecessors (may Allah have mercy on them) regarding rebuttal: that it does not depart from the origin, but what we said is clearer in the aspects of rebuttal, and Allah knows best. And when there is opposition, the way is to give preference. This is the chapter on preference.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the usul principle of 'daf'' (rebutting objections) in analogical reasoning (qiyas). The author explains that when a critic raises an objection to a ruling derived by qiyas, it can be rebutted either by showing the objection does not apply (tard) or by acknowledging the similarity but showing a preventer (mani') that blocks the ruling. The text provides several examples: (1) Urine: if urine does not flow, it is not considered 'external' that necessitates purification, so no talz by analogy. (2) Usurpation (ghasp): it is a cause for ownership of the substitute, but in the case of a mudabbar (slave whose freedom is contingent on master's death), the ruling is prevented by a barrier (the master's ownership), so the analogy is blocked. (3) Attacking camel: destroying it to save oneself is permitted because the necessity of saving life overrides the inviolability of property, and this does not contradict the original ruling on self-defense. (4) Blood of istihadah and continuous wounds: although blood is generally impure and a hadath, the ruling requiring purification is lifted due to continuous need, similar to how limited excuses apply in prayer times. (5) Saying 'amin' quietly: although it is a dhikr and generally should be concealed, adhan and takbirs are exceptions because they serve as announcements, thus have a different ruling. The passage concludes that when objections arise, the method is tarjih (preferring one evidence over another). This discussion is characteristic of Hanafi usul, emphasizing istihsan and careful distinction between genuine objections and mere dissent.

Key Terms
دفع (daf'): Rebutting an objection to a qiyas by showing either that the objection's premise does not apply (tard) or that a preventer (mani') blocks the ruling.
طرد (tard): Consistent application of an analogy without refutation; showing that the objection does not actually contradict the analogy because the same reasoning applies consistently.
نقض (naqd): Refutation or counterexample where the ruling does not apply despite identical cause; the text distinguishes this from tard.
مانع (mani'): A preventer that blocks the legal effect even when the cause exists; e.g., ownership blocking the effect of ghasp.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why does the blood of istihadah not entail the ruling of hadath (requiring wudu) even though it is impure and external?
A) Because it is not actually impure.
B) Because it is not external; it remains inside the body.
C) Because there is a preventer (mani') which is the continuous need and the excuse of time of prayer.
D) Because the analogy fails completely.

Key Takeaway
Daf' is a legitimate Hanafi usul tool to rebut objections by showing either tard or a preventer, preserving the integrity of qiyas.

The 'Illah Itself Is Not a Basis for Tarjih
Rule: conflicting independent causes cannot be used for tarjih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that conflicting independent 'illahs cannot be used for tarjih, and identify the proper basis for tarjih (attributes like 'adala).

Core Idea
Conflicting independent causes cancel each other; tarjih requires attributes, not causes.

In Hanafi usul, when two independent effective causes ('illahs) conflict, neither can be used to give preponderance (tarjih) to one ruling over another. This is because each cause is complete in itself and cannot serve as a weighing attribute. Instead, tarjih must be sought from external attributes such as the reliability (ʿadālah) of a witness. This lesson explores this principle through examples from blood money, pre-emption (shufʿah), and inheritance, and contrasts the Hanafi position with that of al-Shāfiʿī and Ibn Masʿūd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting 'illahs in each example
Examine the passages on blood money, shufʿah, and inheritance. In each, there are multiple potential causes (e.g., multiple wounds, multiple share portions, multiple relationships). Note that each candidate cause is independently sufficient to ground the ruling.
For blood money, each wound could independently cause death. For shufʿah, each part of the share is sufficient to claim the whole. For inheritance, each cousin is independently a cause for taʿṣīb.
2
Recognize that independent 'illahs conflict and cannot be compared
According to the Hanafi rule, when two independent 'illahs conflict, neither can be given preponderance over the other because they are complete causes. They are not attributes that can be weighed; they are co-equal in grounding the ruling.
The author emphasizes that 'each part of the share is sufficient to claim the whole, so conflict occurs with each part however small, and none of it is a description for another.'
3
Apply the rule: conflicting 'illahs do not yield tarjih
Therefore, in each case, the ruling is that both causes are equally valid, and the rights (e.g., pre-emption, inheritance) are shared equally or the blood money is divided equally, not allocated according to the strength of the cause.
For shufʿah, co-owners have equal right regardless of share size. For inheritance, the husband's tie does not give preponderance.
4
Identify where tarjih should be sought instead
The passage concludes: 'tarjih must be sought from the attributes, such as uprightness in the witness and what runs in its course.' Attributes like reliability, not the 'illah itself, are the proper basis for giving preponderance.
This reinforces that tarjih is a matter of evidence evaluation, not of comparing the inherent strength of causes.

Source Text

From them: this is a mistake that blood money is obligatory in halves, and the owner of the wounds is not given preponderance so that he alone is made the killer; because each wound is a conflicting cause, so it cannot be a description by which preponderance occurs. Similarly we said regarding the two pre-emptors in an undivided share sold for two unequal portions: they are equal in entitlement; because each part of the share is sufficient for entitlement to the whole, so the conflict occurs with each part however small, so none of it is a description for another. And al-Shafi‘i, may Allah have mercy on him, agreed with us on this because he did not give preponderance to the owner of the larger share either; but he made pre-emption from the accessories of ownership, like fruit and offspring, so he made it divisible according to the extent of ownership, and this was a mistake from him by making the ruling of the cause generated from the cause and divisible according to its parts. And the jurists are agreed regarding two paternal cousins, one of whom is the husband of the woman: that agnatic inheritance is not given preponderance by the marriage tie; rather each one is considered a cause by itself. And the generality of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, said regarding two paternal cousins, one of whom is a maternal brother: the sixth is for him due to the maternal brotherhood, and the rest is between them by agnatic inheritance, contrary to ‘Abdullah ibn Mas‘ud, and they did not make the maternal brotherhood a preponderating factor because it is a cause by itself, not a description... because it is closer than the paternal unclehood, unlike the maternal brotherhood for a mother, for it was made a description for the paternal brotherhood because this direction is subordinate and the station is one. What is required is seeking preponderance from the attributes, such as uprightness in the witness and what runs in its course.
Source pages p. 645

Commentary
This passage is a rule from Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the 'illah (cause) itself, when it conflicts independently, cannot be used as a basis for tarjih (preponderance). The author first gives an example from blood money: if multiple wounds could each independently cause death, they cannot be used to single out one as the killer. Then the Hanafi position on shuf'ah (pre-emption) is presented: if a property is sold with two shares of unequal value, both co-owners have equal right to pre-empt because each part of the share is a sufficient cause for claiming the whole. The text notes that al-Shafi'i agreed in principle but then mistakenly made shuf'ah a derivative of ownership, dividing it according to share. The author then cites ijma' on inheritance: the husband's tie does not give preponderance in ta'sib. Finally, the Companions' view on two cousins, one a maternal brother, is given: the brother gets a sixth by maternal brotherhood, then both share by agnatic inheritance; the maternal brotherhood is not a preponderating attribute because it is an independent cause. The conclusion: tarjih must be sought from attributes like justice of a witness, not from the 'illah itself.

Key Terms
علة ('illah): The effective cause or ratio legis in legal reasoning; here, an independent cause that grounds a ruling.
ترجيح (tarjih): The process of giving preponderance to one legal evidence or cause over another.
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption in property sales; here used as an example of conflicting causes.
تعصيب (ta'sib): Agnatic succession in inheritance where the male agnate inherits the residue.

C) Because the larger shareholder has a stronger claim based on his greater ownership.
D) Because the seller's consent is assumed to be equal for both.

Key Takeaway
Conflicting independent causes cannot be used for preponderance; tarjih must be based on attributes such as reliability, not on the causes themselves.
