Wiping the Head: Repetition vs Extension
Analyzing the ruling on repeating or extending the head wipe in wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of tab'id (partiality) to derive the ruling on wiping the head in wudu and distinguish between sunnah extension and prohibited repetition.

Core Idea
The Quranic command with partitive bi proves only part of the head is obligatory; extending the wipe is sunnah, repeating it is bid'ah.

This passage delves into a nuanced debate about how to perform the head wipe in wudu. The Hanafi school argues that since the obligation is only to wipe part of the head (as indicated by the ba' in the verse), the sunnah is to extend the wipe rather than repeat it. The passage contrasts this with the arms, where repetition is recommended, and explains why repetition for the head would resemble washing, which is prohibited. This analysis showcases how a subtle linguistic feature (the preposition bi) becomes the basis for a major usul principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
What exactly is being debated? It is about the recommended way to wipe the head in wudu: should one repeat the wipe (tikrar) or extend it (italah)?
The dispute is not about validity but about the preferred sunnah.
2
Locate the primary dalil
What is the Quranic evidence? The verse 'wamsahu bi ru'usikum' (5:6) is cited. The key is the preposition 'bi'.
The dalil is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalala due to the ambiguity of the preposition.
3
Apply the Hanafi usul rule
How does the partitive ba' affect the ruling? Hanafis hold that al-ba' li al-tab'id (the bi indicates partiality), so only a part of the head is required by the text.
This principle is established in the chapters on particles (hurut al-ma'ani).
4
Distinguish from other acts
Why is extension preferred for head but repetition for arms? The head wipe is a light purification (takhfif), while washing (ghusl) is for rational purity. Repeating the head wipe would resemble washing and thus be prohibited.
The argument uses qiyas and istihsan.
5
Formulate the ruling
What is the sunnah and what is bid'ah? The sunnah is to prolong the wipe within its place; repeating the wipe is an innovation (bid'ah) because it adds to the completion beyond the prescribed limit.
This is based on the principle that the original (asl) is to complete by extension, not repetition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل؛ لأن التكرار في الأصل غير مسنون، ولكن المسنون تكميله، وهو الأصل في الأركان، وتكميله بإطالته في محله إن أمكن، بمنزلة إطالة القيام والركوع والسجود ولكن الفرض لما استغرق محله : اضطررنا إلى التكرار خَلَفاً عن الأصل، والأصل ها هنا مقدور عليه في مسح الرأس؛ لاتساع محله فبطل الخلف. وظهر بهذا فقه المسألة، وهو أن لا أثر للركنية في التكرار أصلاً، كما في أركان الصلاة ، ولا أثر لها في التكميل لا محالة، ألا يُرى أن الرأس شاركه مسحُ الخف في الاستيعاب سُنّةً، وهو رخصةٌ، وكذلك المضمضة، فأما المسح : فله أثر في التخفيف لا محالة؛ لأنه لا يؤدي لطهرِ معقول، فلما كان كذلك كانت الإطالة فيه سنةً، لا التكميل بالتكرار. ألا يُرى أن التكميل بالتكرار ربما يُلحقه بالمحظور، وهو الغسل، فكيف يصلح تكميلاً؟ وأما الغسل : فقد شُرع لطهرٍ معقول، فكان التكرار تكميلاً، ولم يكن محظوراً، فقد أدى القول بموجب العلة إلى الممانعة. وهذا كله بناء على أن فرض مسح الرأس يتأدى ببعض الرأس، لا محالة، وذلك غير مسلّم على مذهبهم، بل الفرض يتأدى بكله، ولكن الشرع رخص في الحط إلى أدنى المقادير وذلك كالقراءة عندكم وإن طالت كانت فرضاً، وقد يتأدى بآية واحدة، وإذا كان كذلك : لم يلزمه شيء من هذه الوجوه. والجواب عنه : أن هذا خلافُ الكتاب، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَامْسَحُوا بِرُءُوسِكُمْ﴾ المائدة/6، وقد بينا في أبواب حروف المعاني أن الاستيعاب غير مراد بالنص، فصار البعض هو المراد ابتداء بالنص، فصار أصلاً، لا رخصةً فصار استيعابه تكميلاً للفرض والفضل على نصاب التكميل بدعة، بالإجماع.
The original; because repetition in the original is not recommended, but the recommended is its completion, and that is the original regarding the pillars, and its completion is by prolonging it in its place if possible, like prolonging standing, bowing, and prostration. But when the obligatory act fills its place, we are compelled to repetition as a substitute for the original, and here the original is possible for wiping the head due to the spaciousness of its place, so the substitute becomes invalid. And by this the fiqh of the issue is apparent, which is that there is no effect of pillar-ness on repetition at all, as in the pillars of prayer, and it has no effect on completion necessarily. Do you not see that the head is shared by wiping the khuff in encompassing as sunnah, and it is a concession, and similarly the rinsing. As for wiping, it has an effect in alleviation necessarily, because it does not lead to a rational purity. So when that is the case, prolonging it is sunnah, not completion by repetition. Do you not see that completion by repetition may bring it close to prohibition, which is washing, so how can it be suitable as completion? And as for washing, it is legislated for rational purity, so repetition is completion and is not prohibited. Thus, the statement according to the effective cause leads to obstruction. All this is based on that the obligation of wiping the head is fulfilled with part of the head, necessarily, and that is not conceded according to their school; rather, the obligation is fulfilled with the whole, but the law made a concession to reduce to the minimum, like recitation according to you, if it is long it is obligatory, and it may be fulfilled with one verse. If that is the case, nothing from these aspects is incumbent upon him. The answer to it: This is contrary to the Book. Allah says: {And wipe your heads} [al-Ma'idah 5:6], and we have explained in the chapters on particles that encompassment is not intended by the text. Thus, part is intended initially by the text, so it becomes an original, not a concession. So its encompassment becomes a completion of the obligatory act and excellence; and adding to the limit of completion is innovation by consensus.
Source pages p. 657

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal analysis of the ruling on wiping the head (mas'hu ra's) in wudu, specifically whether one should repeat the wipe or simply extend it. The author, likely from the Hanafi school, argues that the sunnah is to extend the wipe (prolong it) rather than to repeat it, because the basic obligation (fard) can be fulfilled by wiping any part of the head. Repetition is only required when the obligatory act occupies its entire locus, but for the head, the place is large enough that extension suffices. The author draws an analogy to the pillars of prayer where completion is by prolonging, not repeating. The opponents (possibly Shafi'is) argue that the obligation is to wipe the entire head, but the Hanafi response cites the Quranic verse "and wipe your heads" and interprets the preposition "bi" to mean partiality, thus the original obligation is only part. Therefore, covering the whole head is a supererogatory completion, and doing so by repetition would be an innovation (bid'ah) because it resembles washing (ghusl). This illustrates the Hanafi usul principle that the amr (command) with the partitive bi indicates a part, not the whole.

Key Terms
باء (ba'): The preposition 'bi' in the verse indicates partiality (tab'id), meaning only part of the head is required.
استيعاب (isti'ab): Encompassing or covering the entire part (here the whole head).
تكرار (tikrar): Repeating the wiping motion more than once. The text argues it is not recommended for the head.
تكميل (takmil): Completion of the obligatory act by performing the sunnah fully, e.g., extending the wipe.
إطالة (italah): Prolonging the wiping motion within its place, which is the recommended sunnah for wiping the head.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because it resembles washing which is prohibited
C) Because the obligation is to wipe the whole head
D) Because repetition is only for arms

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school derives that wiping part of the head is obligatory from the partitive ba' in the Quranic verse; extending the wipe is sunnah, while repeating it is bid'ah.

Wiping Part of the Head: The Hanafi Usul Argument from the Particle 'Bi'
Usul argument about the implication of the particle 'bi' in the Quranic wiping verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that the particle 'bi' indicates partiality (tab'id) to derive the ruling of partial wiping in wudu, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The Quranic command 'wa imsahu biru'usikum' uses 'bi' to indicate partiality, making partial wiping the original obligation, not a concession.

This lesson dives into a classic Hanafi usul dispute: does the Quranic verse for wudu require wiping the entire head or just part of it? The answer lies in a single preposition 'bi'. By examining the particle's role, the Hanafi school derives that partial wiping is the original obligation, not a relaxation. Understanding this argument sharpens your ability to read Quranic commands with precision and appreciate the depth of Hanafi linguistic methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source text
Locate the operative phrase 'wa imsahu biru'usikum' from Al-Ma'idah 5:6. Focus on the particle 'bi' attached to 'ru'usikum'.
The verse is the primary dalil for wiping the head in wudu.
2
Classify the particle 'bi' according to Hanafi usul
In Hanafi usul, the particle 'bi' (ba') is studied in the chapters on particles of meanings (huruf al-ma'ani). The default classification is that it indicates partiality (tab'id) when attached to a verb of wiping, unless context specifies otherwise.
This is a key principle: al-ba' li al-tab'id.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule to the verse
Since the verse commands wiping (imsahu) and the object is preceded by 'bi', the command is understood to require wiping part of the heads, not the entire head.
Thus, the original obligation (asl) is partial coverage.
4
Derive the ruling
The ruling: wiping part of the head fulfills the obligation of wudu. Comprehensive wiping (isti'ab) is a completion (takmilah) and is not required. Making it compulsory is an innovation (bid'ah) by consensus.
Hanafi position: partial wiping is sufficient.
5
Compare with the opposing school
The Shafi'i school holds that 'bi' indicates contact (iltisaq) not partiality, and they also cite prophetic practice of wiping the entire head. Thus, they require isti'ab. The thamarah al-khilaf: a person who wipes only part has valid wudu according to Hanafis, but not according to Shafi'is.
This difference stems from a usul disagreement on the implication of 'bi'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبِرُءُوسِكُمْ المائدة/٦ ، وقد بينا في أبواب حروف المعاني أن الاستيعاب غير مراد بالنص، فصار البعض هو المراد ابتداء بالنص، فصار أصلاً، لا رخصةً فصار استيعابه تكميلاً للفرض والفضل على نصاب التكميل بدعة، بالإجماع.
And [the verse] 'your heads' [from] Al-Ma'idah 5:6, and we have explained in the chapters on particles of meanings that comprehensiveness is not intended by the text, so part is what is intended initially by the text, so it becomes an original [ruling], not a concession; so its comprehensiveness is a completion of the obligation and virtue upon the limit of completion, an innovation by consensus.
Source pages p. 658

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul argument against the view that wiping the entire head in wudu is obligatory. The author cites the Quranic verse 'and wipe your heads' (Al-Ma'idah 5:6) and states that the particle 'bi' (in 'biru'usikum') indicates partiality, as established in the study of particles. Therefore, the original obligation is to wipe part of the head, not all. Making comprehensive wiping a requirement would be an addition (takmilah) that is considered an innovation (bid'ah) by consensus. This is a classic Hanafi position: the text itself does not demand isti'ab (comprehensive coverage), so partial wiping fulfills the obligation.

Key Terms
استيعاب (isti'ab): Comprehensive coverage; the requirement to wipe the entire head.
باء (ba'): The preposition 'bi' which in this context indicates partiality or adjunction, not full coverage.
تبعيض (tab'id): Partiality; the linguistic quality of indicating a part of something.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi argument, wiping the entire head in wudu is considered what?
A) Fard
B) Sunnah
C) Bid'ah (innovation)
D) Mustahabb

Key Takeaway
The original obligation from the Quranic verse is to wipe part of the head; requiring full coverage is an addition that is not part of the original command.

Definition of Sabab (Cause) in Hanafi Usul al-Fiqh
Defining sabab linguistically and in Shariah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'sabab' as a Shariah term and distinguish it from its linguistic meanings.

Core Idea
Sabab is a means to a ruling, not the ruling itself—like a path to a destination; the walking matters.

In Hanafi legal theory, understanding 'sabab' (cause or means) is foundational because it connects observable events to divine rulings. This lesson unpacks the term from its linguistic roots in the Quran to its precise Shariah definition, clarifying what it is and what it is not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three linguistic uses of sabab
Read the Quranic verses cited: sabab as path (al-Kahf 84-85), as door (Ghafir 36-37), and as rope (al-Hajj 15). Note how each context uses sabab to mean a means to an end.
These are not mere homonyms; they share a core concept.
2
Extract the common meaning
Synthesize the three uses into a single idea: sabab is 'that which is a way or means to something'. This is the linguistic foundation.
The commonality is 'being a means', not the specific object.
3
Formulate the Shariah definition
Understand the Shariah definition: sabab is 'an expression for that which is a means to something: whoever travels it reaches it, and attains it through that journey, not by the path itself.' Distinguish the means from the action taken.
The sabab is the indicator; the ruling is from Allah.
4
Reflect on the example
Consider the analogy of walking a path to Egypt: the path (sabab) is the means, but the walking (action) is what brings arrival. Similarly, a legal sabab (e.g., entering prayer time) does not directly cause obligation; it is the sign linked to the ruling.
This prevents misunderstanding sabab as an efficient cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السبب فإنه يُذكَر ويراد به الطريق، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَءَانَيْنَاهُ مِن كُلِّ شَيْءٍ سَبَبًا فَأَتْبَعَ سَبَبًا﴾ [الكهف 84-85] أي طريقاً. ويُذكَر ويراد به الباب قال الله تعالى: ﴿لَعَلِّي أَبْلُغُ الْأَسْبَابَ أَسْبَابَ السَّمَاوَاتِ﴾ [غافر 36-37] يريد به أبوابها. ومنه قول زهير: ولو نال أسباب السماء بسُلَّم ويُذكر ويراد به الحبل قال الله تعالى: ﴿فَلْيَمْدُدْ بِسَبَبٍ إِلَى السَّمَاءِ ثُمَّ لِيَقْطَعْ﴾ [الحج 15] أي بحبل إلى السقف. ومعنى ذلك كله واحد، وهو ما يكون طريقاً إلى الشيء. وهو في الشريعة: عبارة عما يكون طريقاً إلى الشيء، من سَلَكَه وصل إليه، فناله في طريقه ذلك، لا بالطريق الذي سلك، كمن سلك طريقاً إلى مصر: بَلَغَه من ذلك الطريق، لا به، لكن بمشيه.
As for sabab (cause): it is mentioned and intended to mean 'path'. Allah Most High said: 'And We gave him from everything a sabab, so he followed a sabab' [al-Kahf 84-85], meaning a path. And it is mentioned and intended to mean 'door'. Allah Most High said: 'Perhaps I may reach the asbab, the asbab of the heavens' [Ghafir 36-37], intending by it their doors. And from this is the saying of Zuhayr: 'Even if he reached the asbab of the sky with a ladder.' And it is mentioned and intended to mean 'rope'. Allah Most High said: 'Then let him extend a sabab to the sky, then let him cut' [al-Hajj 15], meaning a rope to the ceiling. The meaning of all of this is one: it is that which is a means to something. And in the Shariah, it is an expression for that which is a means to something: whoever travels it reaches it, and attains it through that journey, not by the path itself, like someone who travels a path to Egypt: he reaches it from that path, not by it, but by his walking.
Source pages p. 681

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the term 'sabab' (cause/means) as used in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author first gives three linguistic uses from the Quran: sabab meaning path, door, and rope—all sharing the concept of a means to an end. Then he provides the Shariah definition: sabab is that which serves as a means to reach something, but the actual attainment is through the action taken, not the means itself. For example, walking on a road to reach Egypt: the road is the means, but the walking is what brings arrival. This distinction is crucial because in legal theory, a sabab is a manifest indicator that the Lawgiver has linked to a ruling, but the ruling itself is from Allah.

Key Terms
السبب (al-sabab): That which is a means or path to something, whether linguistically or in Shariah; in Shariah it is the manifest indicator to which a ruling is linked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Sabab is a manifest indicator that the Lawgiver has linked to a ruling, serving as a means, not a direct cause.

Definition of Shart (Condition) in Hanafi Usul
Defining shart linguistically and in Shariah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of shart linguistically and in Shariah, and explain how it differs from an illah.

Core Idea
A condition (shart) affects existence, not obligation; it is a necessary sign, not a cause.

This lesson defines the technical term 'shart' (condition) as used in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. Understanding shart precisely is critical because conditions often govern the validity of acts, such as wudu for prayer. The text distinguishes shart from illah: shart is a sign without which something does not exist, whereas illah is directly tied to the ruling's obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic meaning
The text states that in Arabic, 'shart' means a necessary sign (al-alamah al-lazimah). Look at the examples: signs of the Hour, conditions in documents, a policeman (as sign of order), and the cupper's mark.
These examples all indicate a sign that is intrinsic or necessary.
2
Identify the Shariah meaning
In Shariah, shart is defined as 'that upon which the existence of something depends, but not its obligation.' This means a condition is necessary for the act to exist (e.g., prayer requires wudu), but the condition itself does not obligate the act.
The key contrast is between existence (wujud) and obligation (wujub).
3
Distinguish shart from illah
An illah (effective cause) is linked to obligation, while a shart is linked only to existence. The text says: 'from the perspective that obligation does not depend on it, it is a sign; from the perspective that existence depends on it, it resembles the illahs, thus it is called shart.'
Shart may sometimes stand in place of illah, as the author promises to explain later.
4
Internalize the examples
Review the given examples: wudu is a shart for prayer (prayer exists only with wudu, but wudu does not obligate prayer). Similarly, covering the awrah is a shart for salat.
Shart is not the cause of obligation; it is a prerequisite for validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشرط: فتفسيره في اللغة العلامة اللازمة، ومنه: أشراط الساعة، ومنه: الشروط للصكوك، ومنه : الشرطي، ومنه: شَرطَ الحَجام. وهو في الشرع : اسم لما يتعلق به الوجود دون الوجوب، فمن حيث لا يتعلق به الوجوب علامة ومن حيث يتعلق به الوجود يشبه العلل فسمي شرطاً، وقد يُقام مَقامَ العلل، على ما نبين إن شاء الله تعالى.

From this are the 'ashrat al-sa'ah (signs of the Hour), and the conditions in documents, and the shurti (policeman), and the cupper's condition. And in the Shariah it is a name for that upon which existence depends, but not obligation. So from the perspective that obligation does not depend on it, it is a sign; and from the perspective that existence depends on it, it resembles the illahs, thus it is called shart. And it may stand in place of illahs, as we will explain, Allah willing.
Source pages p. 682

Commentary
This passage defines 'shart' (condition) linguistically as a 'necessary sign' (al-alamah al-lazimah), giving examples: the signs of the Hour, conditions in contracts, the policeman (whose presence is a sign of order), and the cupper's condition (a mark on the skin). In Shariah, a shart is that upon which the existence of a ruling depends, but not its obligation. For example, wudu is a condition for prayer: prayer does not exist without wudu, but wudu does not obligate prayer. This distinguishes it from an illah, which is linked to obligation. However, a condition sometimes resembles an illah and may be used in its place, as the author promises to explain later.

Key Terms
الشرط (al-shart): A condition: that upon which the existence of something depends, but not its obligation; a necessary sign.

Defining Alamah (Sign) as a Legal Term in Usul al-Fiqh
Defining alamah as a mere sign with no link to existence or obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define alamah and distinguish it from shart, sabab, and illah by applying the Hanafi usul classification.

Core Idea
An alamah is a mere indicator; it neither causes nor conditions a ruling.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, legal indicators are classified into four types: sabab, illah, shart, and alamah. Among these, alamah is the weakest, serving only as a sign of existence without any causal or conditional link to the ruling. This lesson examines the precise definition of alamah and its place in the hierarchy of legal indicators.

Guided Path
1
State the definition
Recall that alamah is 'that by which existence is known, without connection to obligation or existence itself.' This is the core definition.
2
Contrast with shart
Unlike shart, which affects existence (e.g., wudu is a condition for prayer), alamah does not affect existence or obligation.
The text explicitly says alamah is 'below the shart'.
3
Analyze the examples
The milepost indicates direction but does not cause arrival; the lighthouse indicates danger but does not create safety. These illustrate that alamah is a mere sign.
4
Place in hierarchy
Alamah is the weakest of the four: sabab (cause), illah (effective cause), shart (condition), and alamah (sign). It has minimal legal effect.
Memorize this hierarchy: sabab > illah > shart > alamah in terms of legal impact.
5
Apply to a fiqh scenario
Consider dawn (fajr sadiq) as a shart for Fajr prayer, while the adhan is an alamah. The adhan indicates time but does not cause obligation.
This application helps internalize the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العلامة فما يُعرف الوجودَ من غير أن يتعلق به وجوب، ولا وجود، مثل الميل والمنارة، فكان دون الشرط، فهذا تفسير هذه الجملة. وكل ضرب من هذه الجملة منقسم في حق الحكم.
As for al-alamah (sign): it is that by which existence is known, without any connection to obligation or existence itself, like the milepost and the lighthouse. So it is below the shart. This is the explanation of this totality. And every kind of these (four) is divided in relation to the ruling.
Source pages p. 682

Commentary
This passage defines 'alamah' (sign) as the weakest of the four categories. A sign simply indicates the existence of something without being a means (sabab), a cause (illah), or a condition (shart). Examples include a milepost on a road (which shows the way but does not cause arrival) and a lighthouse (which warns of danger but does not create safety). Thus, an alamah is 'below the shart' in legal effect. The author concludes by noting that each of these four types—sabab, illah, shart, and alamah—can be divided in relation to the ruling itself, meaning they apply to different aspects of hukum.

Key Terms
العلامة (al-alamah): A sign that indicates existence without affecting obligation or existence itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Wudu for prayer
D) Intention for prayer

Key Takeaway
An alamah is the weakest legal indicator; it serves only as a sign, not a cause or condition.

Classifying 'Illa: Name, Meaning, and Ruling
Classifying 'illa into four categories based on ism, ma'na, and hukm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a legal cause ('illa) according to the Hanafi usul categories (ism, ma'na, hukm) and identify examples from fiqh.

Core Idea
A legal cause may have name and meaning yet its ruling can be delayed without negating its causal nature — this distinguishes 'illa from sabab.

The Hanafi usul tradition distinguishes between three aspects of a cause: its name (ism), its meaning (ma'na), and its ruling (hukm). Al-Bazdawi introduces a classification of 'illa based on these three criteria, along with a fourth category where the cause resembles a means (sabab). Understanding these categories is essential for correctly analyzing the validity of contracts, obligations, and retroactive effects in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three dimensions of a cause
Identify that every cause has a name (ism) — the term itself, a meaning (ma'na) — the quality that makes it effective, and a ruling (hukm) — the legal consequence. A cause may have all three or only some.
The classification hinges on whether the ruling is immediate, delayed, or absent.
2
Identify the first category: cause in name, meaning, and ruling
This is the typical immediate cause, such as capacity (istita'a) that directly precedes the action. The ruling follows without delay.
Al-Bazdawi implies this category but does not elaborate; it is the default 'illa.
3
Identify the second category: cause in name only
Examples include a conditional obligation (e.g., 'If you enter, you owe a dirham'). The condition is a cause in name because it triggers the obligation, but it lacks full meaning (ma'na) because the obligation is contingent on an external event.
Such a cause does not produce its ruling retroactively.
4
Identify the third category: cause in name and meaning but not in ruling
Examples: suspended sale (bay' mawquf), sale with option (khiyar al-shart), lease contract (ijarah), and any obligation deferred to a future time. These contracts are valid causes in name and meaning, but their ruling (transfer of ownership, obligation to pay) is delayed due to a preventing factor (mani'). Once the preventer is removed, the ruling retroacts to the time of the cause.
Key evidence: when the preventer is removed, the ruling becomes obligatory from the original time, proving it was a true 'illa, not a sabab.
5
Identify the fourth category: cause resembling the means (sabab)
Here the cause exists in name and meaning, but its ruling depends on a separate attribute that develops after the cause. Examples: nisab of zakat (dependent on growth over the year), illness of death (dependent on death), wound (dependent on spread leading to death). The cause is like a means because the ruling is prospective, but it is still a 'illa because the ruling, once established, relates back to the original cause.
Distinction: in the third category, the preventer is external; in the fourth, the preventer is an inherent attribute that must mature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كالاستطاعة مع الفعل عندنا، فإذا تقدمت : لم تُسم علة مطلقة.
وقد فرق بعض مشايخنا بين الفصلين، وقال: لا بد من صفة العلة تقدمها على الحكم، والحكم يعقبها، ولا يقارنها، بخلاف مع الفعل ؛ لأن الاستطاعة عَرَض، لا بقاء لها؛ ليكون الفعل عقيبها، فلضرورة عدم البقاء : تكون مقارنةً للفعل، فأما العلل الشرعية فلها بقاء، وأنها في حكم الأعيان، فيُتصور بقاؤها، ويتراخى الحكم عنها بلا فصل.
٢- وأما الذي هو علة اسماً: فما سبق ذكره من الإيجاب المعلق بالشرط، على ما مرَّ ذكره.
٣- وأما العلة اسماً ومعنىً لا حكماً: فمثل البيع الموقوف هو اسماً؛ لأنه بيع مشروع، ومعنىً؛ لأن البيع لغةً وشرعاً وضع لحكمه، وذلك معناه، لا حكماً ؛ لأن حكمه تراخى لمانع، فإذا زال المانع: ثبت الحكم به من الأصل فيظهر أنه كان علةً، لا سبباً. وكذلك البيع بخيار الشرط علة اسماً ومعنىً، لا حكماً؛ لأن الشرط دخل على الحكم دون السبب ؛ لأن دخول الشرط فيه مخالف للقياس، ولو جعل داخلاً على السبب لدخل على الحكم أيضاً، وإذا دخل على الحكم لم يدخل على السبب، وكان أقلهما أولى، فبقي السبب مطلقاً، فلذلك كان علةً اسماً ومعنىً، لا حكماً. ودلالة كونه علةً لا سبباً ما قلنا إن المانع إذا زال : وجب الحكم به من حين الإيجاب.
٤- وكذلك عقد الإجارة علة اسماً ومعنىً، لا حكماً؛ لما عُرف في موضعه، ولذلك صح تعجيل الأجرة، لكنه يُشبه الأسباب؛ لما فيه من معنى الإضافة حتى لا يستند حكمه. وكذلك كل إيجاب مضاف إلى وقت فإنه علة اسماً ومعنىً، لا حكماً، لكنه يُشبه الأسباب، فكان ذلك من القسم الرابع، وهو العلة التي تُشبه الأسباب، وذلك أن يوجد ركن العلة اسماً ومعنىً، ويتراخى عنه وصفه، فيتراخى الحكم إلى وجوده، وإذا وجد الوصف: اتصل بالأصل لحكمه، فكان بمعنى الأسباب، حتى يصح أداء الحكم قبله. وذلك مثل نصاب الزكاة في أول الحول هو علةٌ اسماً ومعنىً، أما اسماً لأنه وضع له، ومعنىً لكونه مؤثراً في حكمه؛ لأن الغنى يوجب المواساة، لكنه جُعل علة بصفة النماء، فلما تراخى حكمه : أشبه الأسباب. ألا يُرى أنه إنما يتراخى إلى ما ليس بحادث به، وإلى ما هو شبيه بالعلل، ولما كان متراخياً إلى وصف لا يستقل بنفسه : أشبه العلل، وكان هذا الشبه غالباً؛ لأن النصاب أصل، والنماء وصف، ومن حكمه أنه لا يظهر وجوب الزكاة في أول الحول قطعاً، بخلاف ما ذكرنا من البيوع. ولما أشبه العلل، وكان ذلك أصلاً : كان الوجوب ثابتاً من الأصل في التقدير، حتى صح التعجيل، لكن ليصير زكاةً بعد الحول. وكذلك مرض الموت علةٌ لتغير الأحكام اسماً ومعنىً، إلا أن حكمه يثبت به بوصف الاتصال بالموت، فأشبه الأسباب من هذا الوجه، وهو في الحقيقة علة، وهذا أشبه بالعلل من النصاب. وكذلك الجرح علةٌ اسماً ومعنىً لكن تراخى حكمه إلى وصف السراية، وذلك قائم بالجرح، فكان علةً تُشبه الأسباب.
Like capacity (istita'a) with the action according to us; if it precedes, it is not called an absolute cause. Some of our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) distinguished between the two cases and said: It is necessary for the attribute of the cause to precede the ruling, and the ruling follows it, not simultaneous with it, unlike the case with the action; because capacity is an accident (arad) and has no permanence, so that the action may follow it. Due to the necessity of lack of permanence, it becomes simultaneous with the action. As for the legal causes (al-‘ilal al-shar‘iyyah), they have permanence and are like substances, so their permanence is conceivable and the ruling may be delayed from them without separation. 2) As for what is a cause in name only: it is what was mentioned earlier of obligation linked to a condition, as has passed. 3) As for the cause in name and meaning but not in ruling: for example, a suspended sale (al-bay‘ al-mawquf) is a cause in name because it is a valid sale, and in meaning because the sale in language and law is established for its ruling, and that is its meaning, but not in ruling because its ruling is delayed due to a preventing factor; when the preventing factor is removed, the ruling is established from the beginning, so it becomes clear that it was a cause, not a means (sabab). Likewise, a sale with a stipulation of option (al-bay‘ bi-khiyar al-shart) is a cause in name and meaning, not in ruling; because the stipulation enters upon the ruling, not upon the cause; because entry of the stipulation upon it is contrary to analogy, and if it were made to enter upon the cause, it would also enter upon the ruling; and if it enters upon the ruling, it does not enter upon the cause, and the lesser of the two is preferable, so the cause remains absolute; therefore it is a cause in name and meaning, not in ruling. The evidence that it is a cause and not a means is what we said: when the preventing factor is removed, the ruling becomes obligatory from the time of the obligation. 4) Likewise, the contract of lease (al-ijarah) is a cause in name and meaning, not in ruling, as is known in its place; therefore advance payment of rent is valid, but it resembles the means (asbab) because it contains the meaning of addition such that its ruling is not retroactive. Likewise, every obligation added to a time is a cause in name and meaning, not in ruling, but it resembles the means. So that belongs to the fourth category: the cause that resembles the means. That is: the pillar (rukn) of the cause exists in name and meaning, but its attribute is delayed, so the ruling is delayed until its existence; when the attribute exists, it connects to the original (asl) for its ruling; so it is like the means, such that performance of the ruling before it is valid. For example, the nisab of zakat at the beginning of the year is a cause in name and meaning. In name because it is established for it, and in meaning because it is effective in its ruling; because wealth (ghina) necessitates charity, but it is made a cause by the attribute of growth (nama’); when its ruling was delayed, it resembled the means. Do you not see that it is delayed only to something that is not created by it, and to something similar to the causes? And since it is delayed to an attribute that does not stand on its own, it resembles the causes, and this resemblance is predominant; because the nisab is the foundation (asl) and the growth is an attribute (wasf), and it is a ruling that the obligation of zakat does not appear at the beginning of the year definitively, unlike the sales mentioned. And since it resembled the causes and that was the foundation, the obligation is established from the beginning in estimation, so advance payment is valid, but it becomes zakat only after the year. Likewise, the illness of death (marad al-mawt) is a cause for changing rulings in name and meaning, except that its ruling is established by the attribute of connection to death; so it resembles the means from this aspect, yet it is in reality a cause. This is more similar to causes than the nisab. Likewise, the wound (al-jarh) is a cause in name and meaning, but its ruling is delayed to the attribute of spread (saraya), and that is inherent to the wound, so it is a cause that resembles the means.
Source pages p. 690

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul discusses the classification of legal causes ('illa) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author distinguishes four types based on whether the cause has existence in name (ism), meaning (ma'na), and ruling (hukm). The first type (not fully detailed here but implied) is the cause in name, meaning, and ruling—like a cause that immediately produces its effect. The second type is a cause in name only, such as an obligation linked to a condition (e.g., 'if you enter the house, you owe a dirham') — the condition is a cause in name because it triggers the obligation, but it is not a cause in meaning because the obligation is contingent. The third type is a cause in name and meaning but not in ruling: examples include a suspended sale (bay' mawquf), a sale with an option (khiyar al-shart), a lease contract (ijarah), and any obligation added to a future time. These contracts have the essence of a cause (they are valid contracts intended to transfer ownership or benefit), but their legal effect is delayed due to a preventing factor (e.g., suspension, option period, or future time). When the preventing factor is removed, the ruling retroactively applies from the moment of the contract, showing they were causes, not merely means (sabab). The fourth type is a cause that resembles the means (sabab): here the cause exists in name and meaning, but its ruling is delayed until a separate attribute appears. Examples: the nisab of zakat at the beginning of the year (the nisab is the cause, but the ruling of zakat depends on the attribute of growth over the year), illness of death (the illness is a cause for restrictions on bequests and gifts, but the full effect depends on death), and a wound (the wound is a cause for the right of retaliation or diya, but the ruling depends on the spread of the injury leading to death). In these cases, the cause is like a means because the ruling is prospective, but it is still a cause because the ruling, once determined, relates back to the original cause. The passage emphasizes that the distinction affects legal practice, such as the validity of advance payment of rent or zakat before the completion of the period.

Key Terms
العلة (al-'illa): A cause that is effective in establishing a ruling, either immediately or potentially, with name (ism) and meaning (ma'na) but possibly delayed ruling.
السبب (al-sabab): A means that brings about the ruling immediately without retroactive effect, often lacking the full essence of a cause.
المانع (al-mani'): A preventing factor that delays the ruling of a cause, not annulling the cause itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a cause (illa) in name and meaning but not in ruling, according to this passage?
A) The nisab of zakat at the beginning of the year
B) A suspended sale (bay' mawquf)
C) The wound that leads to death
D) The condition in a conditional obligation

Key Takeaway
The classification of 'illa into categories based on ism, ma'na, and hukm is a core Hanafi usul tool that determines retroactivity and the nature of legal causation.

Distinguishing Real from Customary Conditions: The Kitabah Verse
Textual analysis of a Quranic condition to determine its legal weight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a genuine condition in an imperative limits the ruling to recommendation (istihbab) rather than obligation, using the Quranic verse on kitabah as an example.

Core Idea
The condition 'if you know good' in the kitabah verse is real, not customary, and limits the imperative to recommendation.

The Quranic verse on manumission contracts (kitabah) contains a condition that some dismiss as mere custom. This lesson examines why that view is untenable and how the Hanafi school extracts the ruling of recommendation (istihbab) from the imperative. You will learn to distinguish between conditions that affect legal obligation and those that are merely customary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verse and the condition
Locate the specific Quranic verse (Surah an-Nur 24:33) and identify the condition 'if you know good in them'. Recognise that this condition is attached to the imperative 'fakatibuhum'.
The verse is about writing contracts of manumission for slaves.
2
Recognize the opposing claim
Some scholars claim the condition is merely customary (shart 'adatan) and does not affect the ruling. Understand that this view would render the condition meaningless.
The author strongly rejects this, stating the Quran is free from meaningless clauses.
3
Analyse the author's refutation
The author argues that a customary condition would be 'laghw' (vain talk), which is impossible in the Quran. Therefore, the condition must be real and have legal effect.
This is based on the principle that every Quranic clause has purpose.
4
Determine the ruling from the imperative
With a real condition, the lowest degree of the imperative (amr) is recommendation (istihbab), not obligation. The command to write kitabah is thus recommended only when the condition is met.
The author also points to the parallel command 'wa atuhum min mali Allah' which is interpreted as sunnah, reinforcing the same conclusion.
5
Apply the principle to a similar case
Consider another imperative with a condition and practice identifying whether the condition is real or customary, and what ruling results.
This step builds transferable skill.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما قول الله تعالى: ﴿فَكَاتِبُوهُمْ إِنْ عَلِمْتُمْ فِيهِمْ خَيْرًا ) النور / 33: فقد قال بعضهم : هو شرط عادةً، وليس كذلك. 28w وهذا قول بأنه لغو، وكتابُ الله تعالى منزه عن مثله، ولكن أدنى درجات الأمر: استحباب المأمور به واستحباب الكتابة متعلق بهذا الشرط، لا يوجد إلا به، وينعدم قبله. فأما الإباحة فتستغني عنه والمراد بالأمر الاستحباب، ألا يُرى أن قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَءَاتُوهُم مِّن مَّالِ اللَّهِ الَّذِي مَاتَنكُمْ النور / 33: سُنَّةٌ واستحباب
As for the saying of Allah Most High: 'So write (a contract of manumission) for them if you know good in them' (Surah an-Nur 24:33), some said it is a condition customarily, but it is not so. And this is a saying that it is meaningless; the Book of Allah is free from such. But the lowest degree of the command is: recommendation of the commanded thing, and the recommendation of writing (kitabah) is attached to this condition, it does not exist except by it, and is absent before it. As for permissibility (ibahah), it is independent of it, and the intended meaning of the command is recommendation. Do you not see that His saying: 'And give them from the wealth of Allah which He has given you' (Surah an-Nur 24:33) is a sunnah and recommendation?
Source pages p. 696

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the Quranic verse (24:33) regarding the condition 'if you know good in them' for writing a contract of manumission (kitabah). The author rejects the claim that this is a customary condition (shart 'adatan) that is meaningless. Rather, it is a genuine condition that indicates the recommendation (istihbab) of writing the contract. The lowest degree of the imperative here is recommendation, not obligation. Thus, the act of kitabah is recommended only when the condition (knowing good) is present; before that, it is not recommended. Permissibility (ibahah) of writing does not depend on this condition. The author supports this by referencing another part of the same verse where the command to give wealth is interpreted as a sunnah (recommendation).

Key Terms
شرط عادةً (shart 'adatan): A condition that is considered mere custom and not legally effective; the author rejects this for this verse.
استحباب (istihbab): A recommended act, the lowest degree of command; not obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Obligatory when good is known.
B) Recommended only when good is known.
C) Permissible regardless.
D) Forbidden if good is not known.

Key Takeaway
A genuine condition in a Quranic imperative limits its ruling to recommendation (istihbab), not obligation.

Implicit Conditions (Dalalah al-Shart) in Hanafi Usul
Explaining how conditions are indicated implicitly through wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a statement implies a condition (dalalah al-shart) in Hanafi usul and distinguish it from explicit conditions.

Core Idea
A condition is implied when a description follows an indefinite noun, making the ruling contingent.

In Hanafi usul, conditions can be indicated not only by explicit words like 'if' but also implicitly through the structure of a sentence. This lesson explores how a description attached to an indefinite noun (nakirah) triggers a conditional meaning. You will learn to recognize such implicit conditions and understand their legal effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the statement and its components
Break down the example: 'The woman I marry is thrice divorced.' Identify the noun 'the woman' and the description 'which I marry'.
The noun 'al-mar'ah' is indefinite in a generic sense.
2
Classify the description as indefinite or specific
Determine whether the description 'which I marry' refers to a generic category or a specific individual. In the example, it is generic because it refers to any woman he marries in future.
If it said 'this woman I marry', it would be specific.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule: indefinite description implies condition
Since the description is indefinite (nakirah), the statement is interpreted as a condition: the divorce is contingent upon marriage.
The ruling (divorce) is tied to the occurrence of the condition (marriage).
4
Contrast with explicit condition (nass al-shart)
Explicit wording like 'If I marry a woman, she is divorced' combines both condition and result clearly. Implicit conditions achieve the same legal effect through structure.
Hanafi law recognizes both forms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك دلالة الشرط لا تنفك عن مدلوله وذلك مثل قول الرجل : المرأة التي أتزوجها طالق ثلاثاً هذا الكلام بمعنى الشرط دلالةً؛ لوقوع الوصف في النكرة، ولو وقع في العين لما صلح دلالةً، ونص الشرط يجمع الوجهين.
And likewise, the indication of the condition is not separated from its meaning. An example is a man's saying: 'The woman I marry is thrice divorced.' This speech is in the sense of a condition by indication, because the description occurs after an indefinite [noun]; if it occurred for a specific entity, it would not be valid as an indication. And explicit wording of condition combines both aspects.
Source pages p. 697

Commentary
This passage is a rule about how conditions can be indicated implicitly (dalalah) rather than explicitly (sighah). The example given: a man says 'The woman I marry is thrice divorced' — this implies a condition: if he marries a woman, she is divorced. This is understood as a condition because the description 'which I marry' (allati atazawwajuha) is attached to an indefinite noun 'al-mar'ah' (the woman) in a generic sense. If the statement referred to a specific woman (e.g., 'This woman I marry is divorced'), it would not imply a condition; it would be a direct statement about that entity. Explicit wording of condition (nass al-shart) combines both the condition and its meaning explicitly. The Hanafi usul principle here is that implicit conditions are valid when the description is indefinite and generic.

Key Terms
دلالة (dalalah): Indication of a condition through implicit wording, not explicit.
النكرة (al-nakirah): Indefinite noun that allows generic interpretation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'The woman I marry is thrice divorced', why does it imply a condition?
A) Because the word 'talaq' is explicit.
B) Because the description 'whom I marry' is attached to a generic/indefinite noun.
C) Because it mentions three divorces.
D) Because it is spoken by a man.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a statement with a description attached to an indefinite noun implies a condition, making the ruling contingent on the described event.

Shart as Substitute for Cause (Khalaf 'an al-Illa)
Classifying shart as substitute for illa with fiqh examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a condition (shart) substitutes for the cause ('illa) and distinguish it from a direct cause, applying the Hanafi principle of istihsan.

Core Idea
A condition can substitute for a cause when direct attribution is impossible.

This lesson explores the first part of al-Bazdawi's classification of conditions. We examine how a shart can serve as a substitute (khalaf) for the 'illa when the ruling cannot be directly attributed to the cause. The examples of digging a well, inciting a dog, and throwing fire illustrate when liability arises and when it does not based on the intervening free action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal classification
Determine whether the act (e.g., digging a well, inciting a dog) is a condition (shart) or a cause ('illa) for the ruling of liability.
Use the terminology from the passage.
2
Assess the possibility of attribution
Check if the ruling can be directly attributed to the cause. If not, the condition substitutes for the cause.
Example: In digging a well, the ruling cannot be attributed to the digger directly because the victim's free action intervenes.
3
Apply istihsan where appropriate
In disputed cases (like well), Hanafi uses istihsan to prefer the claim of the digger over the guardian, because the condition substitutes for the cause.
Contrast with the wounder (jarh) who cannot claim another cause because he is the owner of the 'illa.
4
Analyze the role of intervening free action
Check if a free action (animal's nature, wind, movement) intervenes between the act and the result. If so, liability is cut off.
Examples: dog veering, beast wandering, wind blowing fire, insects moving.
5
Distinguish between substitution cases
Compare cases where the condition becomes a substitute (well example) vs. where it does not (dog killing game vs. dog killing person).
Hunting is considered a means of earning, so strict analogy applies; for persons, the dog's nature breaks attribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عنه : أن فعل البهيمة لا يُعتبر لإيجاب حكم ما، فأما لقطع الوجوب عنه : فيُعتبر كالكلب يميل عن سَنَن الإرسال، وكالدابة تجول بعد الإرسال، فكذلك هذا.
ولهذا قلنا فيمن حفر بئراً، فوقع فيها إنسان، فهلك، ثم اختلف الولي والحافر، فقال الولي سقط ، وقال الحافر : أسقط نفسه : إن القول قول الحافر ؛ استحساناً؛ لما قلنا إن الحفر شرطٌ جُعل خَلَفاً عن العلة لتعذر نسبة الحكم إلى العلة، فإذا ادعى صاحب الشرط أن العلة صالحة لإضافة الحكم إليها : فقد تمسك بالأصل، وجَحَدَ حكماً ضرورياً، فجعلنا القول قوله.
بخلاف الجارح إذا ادعى الموت بسبب آخر : لم يُصدق؛ لأنه صاحب علة.
ولهذا قلنا في الجامع الصغير فيمن أشلي كلباً على صيد مملوك، فقتله، أو على نفس فقتلها، أو مزق ثياب رجل: لم يضمن شيئاً؛ لأنه صاحب سبب، وقد اعترض عليه فعل مختار غير مضاف إليه ؛ لأن الكلب يعمل بطبعه وليس الذي أشلاه بسائق.
بخلاف ما إذا أشلى على صيد، فقتله فإن صاحبه جعل كأنه ذبحه بنفسه؛ لأن الاصطياد من المكاسب في الجملة، فبني على نفي الحرج، وقَدْرِ الإمكان، ووَجَبَ المصير في ضمان العدوان إلى محض القياس. ولهذا قلنا فيمن ألقى ناراً في الطريق، فهبَّت بها الريح، ثم أحرقت: لم يضمن.
وإذا ألقى شيئاً من الهوام في الطريق، فتحركت، وانتقلت، ثم لدغت: لم يضمن.
وبعض هذه المسائل تُخرَّج على ما سبق في باب تقسيم الأسباب، ملحقة بذلك الباب.
٤- وأما الذي هو شرط اسماً، لا حكماً : فإن كل حكم تعلق بشرطين : فإن أولهما شرط اسماً، لا حكماً ؛ لأن حكم الشرط أن يضاف الوجود إليه، وذلك مضاف إلى آخرهما، فلم يكن الأول شرطاً، إلا اسماً. ولهذا قلنا فيمن قال لامرأته : إن دخلت هذه الدار، وهذه الدار فأنت طالقٌ، ثم أبانها، ثم دخلت إحداهما، ثم نكحها، ثم دخلت الثانية: إنها تطلق عندنا خلافاً لزفر رحمه الله ؛ لأن الملك شرط عند وجود الشرط ؛ لصحة وجود الجزاء، لا لصحة وجود الشرط ولم يوجد ها هنا جزاء يفتقر إلى الملك، فلم يجز أن يُجعل الملك شرطاً لعين الشرط؛ لأن عينه لا تفتقر إلى الملك، ولم يجز شرطه لبقاء اليمين، كما قبل الشرط الأول.
٥- وأما الشرط الذي هو علامة: فالإحصان في باب الزنا.
وإنما قلنا إنه علامة؛ لأن حكم الشرط أن يمنع انعقاد العلة إلى أن يوجد الشرط، وهذا لا يكون في الزنا بحال؛ لأن الزنا إذا وجد لم يتوقف حكمه على إحصان يحدث بعده، لكن الإحصان إذا ثبت: كان معرفاً لحكم الزنا، فأما أن يوجد الزنا بصورته فيتوقف انعقاده علةً على وجود الإحصان : فلا، فثبت أنه علامة، وليس بشرط، فلم يصلح علة للوجود، ولا للوجوب، ولذلك لم يُجعل له حكم العلل بحال.
ولذلك لم يَضمن شهود الإحصان إذا رجعوا على كل حال، بخلاف ما تقدم في مسألة الشرط الخالص.
And the answer to it: that the act of an animal is not considered for establishing any ruling, but for cutting off obligation from him: it is considered, like the dog that veers from the path of sending, and like the beast that wanders after sending, so likewise this. And because of this we said regarding the one who dug a well, and a person fell into it and perished, then the guardian and the digger disputed: the guardian said he fell, and the digger said he threw himself down: that the saying is the saying of the digger, by istihsan; because we said that digging is a condition that is made a substitute for the cause due to the impossibility of attributing the ruling to the cause. So when the owner of the condition claims that the cause is suitable for adding the ruling to it, he has clung to the original and denied a necessary ruling, so we make his saying the saying. Contrary to the wounder when he claims death due to another cause: he is not believed, because he is the owner of the cause. And because of this we said in al-Jami' al-Saghir regarding the one who incited a dog upon owned game, and it killed it, or upon a person and it killed him, or tore a man's clothes: he does not guarantee anything; because he is the owner of a cause, and a free action that is not attributed to him intervened; because the dog acts by its nature and the one who incited it is not a driver. Contrary to the case when he incited it upon game and it killed it, then its owner is made as if he slaughtered it himself; because hunting is among the means of earning in general, so it was built on removal of hardship and to the extent possible, and it became necessary to resort in the guarantee of transgression to pure analogy. And because of this we said regarding the one who threw fire in the road, and the wind blew it and then it burned: he does not guarantee. And if he threw some insects in the road, and they moved and transferred and then stung: he does not guarantee. And some of these issues are derived from what preceded in the chapter on division of causes, attached to that chapter. 4- And as for that which is a condition in name, not in ruling: every ruling that is attached to two conditions: then the first of them is a condition in name, not in ruling; because the effect of the condition is that existence is attributed to it, and that is attributed to the last of them, so the first is not a condition except in name. And because of this we said regarding the one who said to his wife: If you enter this house, and this house, then you are divorced, then he separated her, then she entered one of them, then he married her again, then she entered the second: that she is divorced in our view, contrary to Zufar; because ownership is a condition at the existence of the condition for the validity of the existence of the consequence, not for the validity of the existence of the condition. And here there is no consequence that requires ownership, so it is not permissible to make ownership a condition for the essence of the condition; because its essence does not require ownership, and it is not permissible to condition it for the remaining of the oath, as before the first condition. 5- And as for the condition that is a sign: it is ihsan in the chapter of zina. And we only said it is a sign; because the effect of the condition is to prevent the causation from taking effect until the condition occurs, and this does not happen in zina at all; because when zina occurs, its ruling does not depend on ihsan that happens after it. But when ihsan is established: it is a definer of the ruling of zina. As for the existence of zina in its form, that its taking effect as a cause depends on the existence of ihsan: no. So it is established that it is a sign, not a condition, and it is not suitable as a cause for existence nor for obligation, and therefore it is not given the ruling of causes at all. And therefore the witnesses of ihsan do not guarantee at all if they retract, contrary to what preceded in the issue of the pure condition.
Source pages p. 701

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Bazdawi (Kanz al-Wusul) presents a classification of conditions (shurut) in Hanafi legal theory. The author begins by discussing the act of an animal: such an act is not considered to impose a ruling (e.g., liability), but it can negate pre-existing obligation. For example, if a dog veers from the direction it was sent, or an animal wanders after being sent, that behavior cuts off the liability of the sender. Then he addresses the case of digging a well: if a person digs a well and someone falls in, the digger is not liable if the victim intentionally threw himself in, because digging is a condition (shart) that substitutes for the cause ('illa) when the 'illa cannot be directly applied. Here, istihsan gives the digger the benefit of the doubt. This contrasts with a direct injurer (jarh) who cannot claim the death was due to another cause because he is the owner of the 'illa. Next, the author discusses inciting a dog: if a dog kills owned game or a person, the inciter is not liable because the dog acts by its own nature (a free action intervenes). However, if the dog kills game, the inciter is treated as if he slaughtered it himself because hunting is a form of acquisition, and liability is based on strict analogy (qiyas). Similarly, throwing fire in the road or releasing poisonous insects does not create liability if the wind or movement causes the harm, because a free action of nature intervenes. These examples are drawn from the chapter on division of causes. Then the author introduces the fourth type: condition in name only (shart isman). When a ruling depends on two conditions, the first is a condition only in name because the existence of the ruling is attributed to the last condition. The example: a man says to his wife, 'If you enter this house and this house, you are divorced,' then separates her, she enters one, he remarries her, then she enters the second. According to Abu Hanifa, she is divorced upon entering the second, contrary to Zufar who would require ownership (nikah) at the time of the first condition. The Hanafi position: ownership is only required for the validity of the consequence (the divorce), not for the condition itself. Since no divorce occurs at the first entry, ownership need not exist then. The fifth type is the condition as a sign ('alama), exemplified by ihsan (having been in a valid marriage) in the law of zina. Ihsan is not a true condition because it does not delay the effect of zina as a cause; instead, it defines the ruling (i.e., stoning vs. flogging). If the witnesses of ihsan retract, they do not guarantee (pay blood money) because ihsan is only a sign, not a cause. This contrasts with a pure condition where retraction would entail liability.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A condition; an external factor that affects the existence or obligation of a ruling, here specifically as a substitute for the cause.
علة (illa): The effective cause (ratio legis) to which the ruling is attributed directly; in the well example, digging is a condition that substitutes for the illa when attribution is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the well example, why does the Hanafi school accept the digger's claim that the victim jumped?
A) Because the digger has witnesses.
B) Because digging is a condition that substitutes for the cause, and istihsan supports the digger's claim.
C) Because the victim was trespassing.
D) Because the digger is the owner of the land.

Key Takeaway
A condition that substitutes for the cause (khalaf 'an al-illa) changes liability rules, often allowing istihsan to override qiyas.
